0316066524|5.0|Another astute reviewer called Infinite Jest the ugliest American masterpiece, and I think that's incredibly apt and precise. It is this lurching, shaggy behemoth born of DFW's obssession that checkout-line-tier junk and acclaimed literary fiction of the period--the touchstones of low and high culture--had essentially the same goal: compel the reader toward the end and along the way supercharge them with accumulated feeling or insight or appreciation for fine craft. He found it toxic. His goal was more audacious, more prescriptive: to give the reader tools to prise that gilded cage apart, to be a more conscious reader with the hope that the resultant rigor would seep into other areas of life.<br /><br />Know that it is a rigorous book. Know that the ugliness is tightly and expertly controlled, not mere sloppiness. Know that all the pleasures you'll get from this novel will be oblique in one way or another because that is the point. Know that there IS an ending, just not an Aristotelian, Shakespearean, or even postmodern one. Know that you do get a few hundred pages of connective tissue, which is satisfying in a way completely divorced from plotting considerations. I cannot say Infinite Jest was enjoyable or entertaining--it was, in dense, explosive clusters that definitely did not comprise more than half the novel. Here's what it was for most of it's length: vivid, interesting, rigorous, and most importantly, finely felt. This is different from deeply or profoundly felt, which can be captured in Realist terms, with gorgeous, crisp prose.<br /><br />Wallace sculpts inner lives in the abstract, with heaps of finely observed cuttings of information, emotion, syntax, and subtext laid alongside each other which crackle with intensity, all the more astonishing because this laser-like empathy shoots from these UGLY, sedimentary walls of text, and (at least I thought), \\"How is this possible? I spend much of the time flipping back and forth to sometimes infuriatingly factualminutiae, in the dictionary, or puzzling out tenuous connections. How could he get me to care about these characters' inner lives so minutely when the foregoing work erodes some of the pleasure I get from reading?\\" It's because the work is bound up in the book as part of Wallace's (cutting edge/old fashioned) moral approach to fiction. If Infinite Jest can inhabit you (I had to pick very carefully here--it is not only a matter of entertaining, arresting, appreciating, astonishing, or even finishing--as these all have their narcotizing aspects if engaged unconsciously) you will eventually be enriched by it. Maybe not immediately, but surely you will. Surely and eventually is better than immediately but contingent, right? And if, after all of this qualified endorsement, you're still an interested skeptic--give it two tries, maximum. I'll be frank and admit I'm contemptuous of the many one-starred reviews that can blast the book in a few sentences and slip in somewhere that they finished in a week or two. Or those tortured souls who've read the same 100 or so pages 3,4,5+ times over. Why try to turn somersaults with one hand nailed to the floor?<br /><br />I hope you'll give Infinite Jest your time. It is even more deserving of your native intelligence and empathy.

Having read \\"The Introvert Advantage\\" by Marti Olsen Laney and \\"Introvert Power\\" by Laurie Helgoe, I was wondering if there's going to be another book about introversion saying something that isn't more or less repeating what another author already said.<br /><br />\\"Quiet\\" did deliver in that it was an exhaustive, scholarly discussion of the subject, complete with stories from public figures both past and present who are introverts. However, I found it too wordy, too detailed, too intellectual. And maybe that's why it wasn't my thing in a book. There is a big difference between right-brained and left-brained introverts. I'm glad I was able to find \\"Quiet\\" at the library and didn't spend money to read it.

B008B0QH5S|5.0|Pet Shop Boys' last album, Yes, was rather lackluster.  It had some good moments, but overall it seemed a bit flat.  Before that, Fundamental was pretty good yet still failed to live up to the truly great albums like Very, Behaviour, or even Release.  So it was with a cautious ear that I listened to their latest offering, Elysium.  I am pleased to report that this album redeems all past mistakes.  PSB has crafted a truly blissful offering filled with intelligent and mature electropop.<br /><br />The most striking aspect of Elysium that most people will notice is that the album isn't a four-on-the-floor dance record.  It's not Nightlife.  In fact, it's closer to a combination of Behaviour and Release.  It's got pop ballads and mid-tempo gems, but nothing for clubs.  This isn't a bad thing...as PSB seems to really nail it when they go this route.  I read some of the other reviews and it seems many were disappointed because they were expecting something more upbeat.  But if one listens without expectations, they will be pleasantly surprised.  There isn't a single track on this album that I want to skip, which is nice as the last album was so sketchy.<br /><br />The best tracks on here are definitely \\"Leaving\\", which has some really chic arrangement, \\"Invisible\\" is melodic and heartfelt, \\"Breathing Space\\" is like a more accessible version of Release's \\"Birthday Boy\\", \\"Ego Music\\" is erratic and experimental, reminiscent of something you'd hear from the French duo Air, but still works wonderfully here.  In fact, starting with \\"Ego Music\\" the album delves into full concept album mode.  Mixing broadway-style numbers like \\"Hold On\\" with the gloriously dark and abstract \\"Everything Means Something\\".  Truly mesmerizing stuff!<br /><br />This is truly the Pet Shop Boys' most beautiful and lush album to date.  I truly hope the people giving it negative reviews will click to this album.  It truly cannot be missed.

B00466H3F4|5.0|Babe was a surprise contender for Best Picture in 1995. It has elements which make it sound like a formula for a cloyingly sweet kiddie film, namely talking animals, a pig that wants to be a sheepdog, singing mice, a farmer who seldom talks paired with a wife who seldom stops talking.  But the component parts were so skillfully crafted, so lovingly combined into a heart-warming and tear-inducing modern fairy tale, that they became a favorite movie for millions of people, a movie which will stand the test of time.<br /><br />The Blu-ray edition is an improvement over the DVD mostly because the resolution is increased for proper presentation on today's larger television screens. Fine detail is good but not spectacular, colors and contrasts are sharp.  The talking animals are consistently lifelike and smoothly believable.  Voice characterizations are remarkably well done, particularly Babe, the old sheep Maa, the sheepdogs Rex and Fly, and the self-described \\"anorexic duck\\" named Ferdinand.<br /><br />James Cromwell is Farmer Hoggett, who wins a piglet at the fair.  Because Hoggett is a man of very few words, playing the character made Cromwell worry that he wasn't really acting.  Director Chris Noonan assured him that he was doing more acting without words than most actors did with words, and his Oscar nomination for Best Supporting Actor proved the point.<br /><br />The simple story is told with charm and emotion. The lyrically pastoral setting has at its center a farmhouse with an almost fantasy appearance, occupied by the farmer and his wife who are a comically matched pair of opposites.  And the animals are amazing, with the Jim Henson creatures being so lifelike that you have to watch the film two or three times to tell the mechanical creations from the live animals.<br /><br />Watch Babe with the kids.  Or maybe watch it on your own, so they don't see the moistening of your eyes when the little pig is told by the mean spirited cat that pigs are only kept by humans for use as food; or when Farmer Hoggett sings Babe out of his depression; or when the two of them together triumph at the sheepdog trials.  This is filmmaking that restores a sense of magic to the craft, and creates a story worth visiting again and again.
B008LZHA3G|4.0|I first heard Bob Dylan when I was 12 years old, too many years ago. I can still remember my reaction- I could not believe how grating the voice I heard over the radio sounded. Well, that was many years ago and over time I not only came to appreciate Dylan's voice, but I believe that in many of his songs, his vocal talent is without parallel in its delivery, timing, and deep emotional expression.<br /><br />As much as I love Dylan, his voice apparently is showing the strain from too many concerts and too many cigarettes. Most of the time he sounds like he is gargling, rather than singing, or more like Louie Armstrong (another artist I love).<br /><br />The bottom line to the Tempest, his latest album, is that he once again shows his mastery, but it is not for everyone.<br /><br />Dylan is by far my favorite musician and songwriter (the music I would take to a desert island if I could only bring one artist).  If you are new to Bob Dylan, I would not start with this album but listen to his earlier work first.  Those who write this may be one of his best albums either have not listened to his earlier albums, or else they listened so long ago (Dylan's been around and so have I) that they forgot.<br /><br />Dylan's range and creativity have decreased over the years. There was a time, beginning with Highway 61 Revisited, then with John Wesley Harding, Nashville Skyline, and others, when it was hard to believe that the same Dylan did the album as the one before.<br /><br />Now, since Time Out of Mind in 1997 Dylan has been on what is probably his longest stretch of similarity. The Tempest breaks no new ground, but continues in the vein of his recent work.<br /><br />Dylan used to write unique and timeless songs, such as Desolation Row, Joker Man, Changing of the Guards ....  the list goes on and on, and is long. Now, in this album \\"Long and Wasted Years\\" sounds like a weaker sequel to Brownsville Girls. \\"Tin Angel\\" reminds me of Man with a Long Black Coat. The sequel is rarely as good as the original, and ever since Time Out of Mind, Dylan's albums have had a more sequel-like quality, at least for me.<br /><br />The title song \\"Tempest\\", all 14 minutes, simply does not rise to the glory of other extended Dylan songs such as Hurricane, Desolation Row, Sad Eyed Lady and others.<br /><br />Of course, Dylan at a mildly disappointing level still overshadows most songwriters at their best. Dylan's \\"average\\" work would be another artist's tour de force. His music always contains insights and imagery that ring true and bears at least additional listening. That is true for this album as well.<br /><br />There are many reviews analyzing specific songs. For me, Pay in Blood and Scarlet Town are great songs with great lyrics and presence. Most of the songs are ones I will listen to frequently. I was expecting not like the song, the Tempest (I could not quite get why Dylan wanted to write about a topic that has been way over explored), I will say this: although the music gets monotonous fairly quickly, in some respects it is one of his better pieces of poetry. The twist he gives to the imagery with the watchman at the end of the song (I will not give it away here), was in my view one of his cleverest lyrical moves in a long time.<br /><br />Dylan continues to bring vivid imagery and depth to his writing.I am also a fan of his rhyming abilities (who else could create a powerful image rhyming \\"pronounce\\" with \\"ounce\\"?).<br /><br />There are great lyrics in this album, lots of religious imagery, but I am disappointed that Dylan does not play harmonica on a single song. I think Dylan is the most creative powerful harmonica player I have ever heard...<br /><br />As a long time Dylan fan and one who listens to his music on a regular basis, one thing I know for sure that when it comes to Dylan, he is usually one step ahead and sometimes it takes me a while to appreciate his work. This is a compliment, because it is a further testament to the depth of his work that we who listen have to work ourselves to fully appreciate him.<br /><br />Dylan's new work is always compared to what he has done before. Since he has done so much, Dylan cannot always live up to expectations and he never has (since he has almost always been five steps ahead of everyone else on the music scene). I hope this is not his last album, and that next time he throws away his canvas from the last 15 years and surprises himself and us with something extraordinary and different. If not, that's still okay, because Dylan has given so much to the world he is actually entitled to rest on his laurels.

The main idea of this book consists of morals and life lessons from a catholic man's point of view.<br /> The author, Paulo Coelho, uses Santiago to express his opinions and morals. Throughout the book, Santiago's many conversations and actions teach readers the value of patience, dedication, and many other virtues. One example comes from a scene where Santiago is saying goodbye to a girl he loves named Fatima. Fatima tells Santiago she will wait for him while he searches for his treasure and will not give up hope. Also, the author shows readers how important it is to take advantage of the opportunities you get and to pursue your dreams through Santiago's actions.<br /> Another thing I enjoyed about this book is that it was broad enough so that all types of people could interpret the symbols and lessons however they wanted.  The Alchemist is simple and to the point. It was open ended so that readers who were willing to dive deep into Coelho's words could understand and enjoy its complexity. On the other hand, readers who just wanted entertainment got an enjoyable, relaxing, quick read. However, there are parts of the book that were too unrealistic for me to take seriously and that I did not enjoy. One scene is where Santiago tries to turn himself into the wind and has conversations with the desert and sun. B008LZHA3G|5.0|Let's face it, even an icon has his ups & downs. Not all that glitters is gold. But think Dylan has stuck the mother lode this time.If Modern Times was another classic and Together Through This Life a bit of a let down but far from a train wreck, Tempest shows Dylan shaking off the chill of going not so gently into that not so good night.<br /><br />Duquesne Whistle is a jaunty, haunting opener, self-consciously invoking a bygone era. The gorgeous Soon After Midnight harks back to the likes of Bye & Bye, Moonlight or Beyond the Horizon. But if things seem to be going the way of wistful reverie, skies get redder and clouds darker with the hypnotic Narrow Way.<br /><br />If Early Roman Kings owes a bit too much to Mannish Boy, it's hardly a first if you go back to the beginning of Dylan's career. Amateurs borrow professionals steal. Musically, he's only doing what the likes of Muddy Waters and Robert Johnson did long before him. Granted Dylan never had their chops on the guitar but that's missing the point. Meanwhile, the band here are no slouches. And while they may lack the inspired mayhem that brought us Blonde on Blonde or Highway 61, they respectfully set the scene for this dark, restless album.<br /><br />On Long & Wasted Years, his rusted out pipe of a voice has never been so effective. Elsewhere, Pay In Blood is the most pissed off I've heard Dylan in years and this where the body count starts to rise. Other standouts include the ominous, brooding Scarlet Town and Tin Angel, proof Dylan's narrative skills have yet to wane.<br /><br />The ambition of the title cut cannot be denied. A maze-like narrative with a tender thread to see you through to the other side. Whether he's talking about the ship of state or a state of mind, or both Dylan lets you choose from among many metaphors here. And while I'm willing to stand corrected, I believe this clocks in as the longest song he's ever cut in the studio.<br /><br />Leave it to Dylan to end the album with an unexpected lump in the throat. Roll On John is a fond, if strangely belated farewell to John Lennon. Delivered some 32 years after his death, its hard to tell whether a little self-mockery plays a part or not. Regardless, it's a moving coda, painting a complex portrait of a now revered figure.<br /><br />If the career renaissance that began with Time Out of Mind ends here, Tempest is a hell of an Elephant Graveyard. If anything it leaves the future open to question while at the same time questioning the past. \\"I ain't dead yet, the bells still ring. I keep my fingers crossed like the early Roman Kings.\\"<br /><br />While certain to have it's detractors, Tempest is another strong effort. B008LZHA3G|5.0|Dear Music Appreciators,<br /><br />I finally started to pay closer attention to Bob Dylan's music after seeing the documentary NO DIRECTION HOME on DVD.  I don't know why it took me so long - maybe sometimes a person just needs the right guide, especially when they're traveling in a world as rich and complex as Bob Dylan's (thank you Martin Scorsese).  And now here comes TEMPEST, the first proper studio album since I officially became a Bob Dylan fan - and it was worth waiting for.<br /><br />Opener \\"Duquesne Whistle\\" will inject any room with a little something I like to call \\"Instant Atmosphere.\\"  The gently twanging opening riffs transport the listener back in time to the heyday of western swing king Bob Wills, but when the band kicks it up a notch at the forty-two second mark, it isn't long before this chugging old-timey train song takes on a weird intensity all its own - check out the official video appearing on sites like Amazon and You Tube and see what kind of strangely romantic and violent images this song begat in at least one director's mind.<br /><br />\\"Soon After Midnight\\"  sounds like a fifties-style slow-dance swooner that might even get my fiercely anti-Dylan wife to sway around the room with me, while still delivering Dylanesque delights like \\"I`m searchin` for phrases, to sing your praises,\\" which contrasts amusingly with a triple headed rhymer like \\"Charlotte's a harlot, dresses in scarlet.\\"<br /><br />\\"Narrow Way\\" employs a searing, broken-record style blues riff for over seven minutes while Dylan growls his way through a winding tale of apocalyptic anxiety and violence, punctuated by the memorable refrain \\"if I can't work up to you, you'll surely have to work down to me some day.\\"<br /><br />And it just gets better from there.<br /><br />Many casual and new Dylan listeners might find the first half of the album to be the most user-friendly, while tracks 6-10 contain the more challenging (and more violent) offerings, including the fourteen minute, forty-five verse title track as well as a tribute to the late John Lennon.  But this is all worthwhile stuff delivered with a brilliant confidence and DIY flair appropriate to one of the most important and influential figures in the history of recorded music.  The fans and the critics will get this album and will have plenty to say about it, but to the Bob Dylan fence sitters I'd like to quote Joan Baez in NO DIRECTON HOME:<br /><br />\\"There are no veils, curtains, doors, walls, anything between what pours out of Bob's hand onto the page and what is somehow available to the core of people who are believers in him.  \\"There's some people who'd say... you know, not interested.  But if you're interested, he goes way, way deep.\\"<br /><br />Hopefully this outstanding new album will find many of those who are interested...but just don't know it yet.<br /><br />Sincerely,<br /><br />Constant Listenere, forty-five verse title track as well as a tribute to the late John Lennon. B008OGJXJ6|5.0|The matter that makes up Dylan's 35th outing is dark and complex; beginning in 1997 (Time out of Mind), this is possibly his final creative outburst - and what a trip it's been!  History will compare such incredible body of work with the likes of Cohen and Tagore, in terms of holding on an edge, desire (and sanity) until latter years of one's mortal life.<br /><br />Tempest is Dylan's 5th masterpiece in a row - perhaps one that will win most 10/10s in critical reviews  since 2001's Love and Theft (incidentally that was also released on Sep 11); the final years now likely rivals his most prolific period between 1963 and 1966 spanning six albums. Six sheer classics and more have been written on them than you'd have time to read.<br /><br />That's past; a foundation of popular culture. Let's talk about the present here.<br /><br />This is an album of our times - a portrait of  decline, a reflection of decadence, in society, in families and life in general; this is part and parcel of our cultural landscape that looks more & more like a vacant parking lot, sky full of fire and pain pouring down - not my words here - livelihoods bust, businesses bankrupt and enough bullets in supply to eradicate the entire human race; rich  getting richer and poor gets poorer, `grinding penury and exceeding luxury\\" side by side - on and off Wall Street. \\"I will pay in blood but not my own' he says, and means every word of it.<br /><br />I moved to the west in early nineties; few years later, the unplugged  sessions came out and I thought that would be his last album; revisiting that recording, I now sense a sense of restlessness - a desire to stand up and sing ...<br /><br />Tempest is like an epilogue to 'Not Dark Yet', not musically, rather thematically; it's dark now - he knows that; and he wraps every word of this album in that darkness; mind you this is a dark album, not brooding or melancholic. Light flickers, occasionally, like in the intro of Duquesne Whistle - elsewhere, that light is not abundant. This is as far away as Dylan can be from Modern Times - standing up and singing close to the microphone - sometimes in an angry growl, sometimes a murmur or a prayer.<br /><br />The backing band swings with force of a gale; better than The Band, and gets a same respectful nod from this writer as to the man himself; listening to the album, I get the vision of a flock of seagulls flying in unison, like multiple sound waves traveling together but distinctly separate in character from each other.<br /><br />The recording is clear, dense and spontaneous - far from the atrocious digital quality you get these days  - almost sounds like an analog recording-  like the best of the Hi Fi when, say, Highway 61 got released (just keep wishing there was a monaural version available!)  This is not an album that one should listen to on a headphone or in mp3 - you need a player, a physical disc and couple of speakers. Then sit tight - I promise its gonna be a great ride!<br /><br />The first five or six reviews that I read on the Amazon US website are excellent, with deep insights - written with care and will form valuable guidance to appreciative readers for years to come. In addition, both UNCUT and Mojo reviews are works of art in itself.<br /><br />Musically, this is perhaps Dylan's best effort since Oh Mercy. That was mostly Daniel Lanois creating a mystical dreamy landscape - this time its Jack Fate at the wheels - bringing out the best in every member in his gifted band and every word that Dylan has written for this album. The timing, the nuance, the utter simplicity & effortless delivery - all remain superb and reminiscent of a man in his prime. In my opinion this album will reveal much more in terms of meaning, music and depth over time, over repeated listening - something I have found with Blonde on Blonde and John Wesley Harding, among others - they still talk to me like never before.<br /><br />Dylan's singing is in perfect pitch that is best suited for his own writings; these songs are not rushed (like I felt, in parts, for Together Through Life) - they are impeccably constructed with absolutely no fluff or excess. These are songs only he can sing - and his voice, the angry growl and loving whisper remains as unrepeated in the past as this album in its entirety.<br /><br />The skies are dark, the air is dirty, families, people, races and religions are all disconnected. Perfect! What better time to give Tempest another spin!<br /><br />Thank you again, Seor!

1401322786|4.0|Mitch Albom's latest work of fiction opens with an evocative scene. An ancient man, solitary in a cave, listens to the voices of people from Earth begging endlessly for one thing: more time.<br /><br />The precise identity of this man isn't known for some time; instead, the novel introduces readers to two of the people whose voices the man hears. One is teenager Sarah Lemon. She longs for more time to prepare for her first date, with a handsome and seemingly unattainable boy who volunteers at the same homeless shelter with her on the weekends. Ethan is flirtatious but can be aloof, and Sarah can't quite believe that he's interested in her --- awkward Sarah Lemon, with her divorced parents, overachieving grades, and few extra pounds. She wants the night to be perfect, though, and for that she needs more time. But what if Ethan has something else in mind for their time together?<br /><br />Not as much time, though, as Victor Delamonte desires. Victor, a hugely successful and wildly wealthy hedge fund manager, is used to getting everything he wants. So when he discovers that he's dying of cancer, he doesn't believe that he can't just buy his way into immortality. Soon, though, he discovers cryonics and hatches an elaborate plan to extend his life decades or centuries into the future. As he grasps for a miracle, he finds himself growing farther and farther away from his once-beloved wife. Isn't his remaining time with her worth just as much as some nebulous quest for eternal life?<br /><br />And what about that mysterious, ancient man, forced to listen to the stories of Sarah, Victor, and millions of others just like them? It turns out that he's the one who first sparked humans' peculiar fixation with time. Dor, who lived near the time of the building of the Tower of Babel, was the first man to conceptualize the passing --- and the measurement --- of time. He invented the first clock, and from then on, Albom's book suggests, humans never stopped fretting about time passing too slowly or, more often, too quickly. Although Dor gained a sort of cursed immortality, it was at the price of everything he held most dear. Now he mourns his losses, sitting in that cave, alone but for the voices that surround him, which are their own form of punishment: \\"to hear every plea from every soul who desired more of the thing he had first identified, the thing that moved man further from the simple light of existence and deeper into the darkness of his own obsessions.\\"<br /><br />The ways in which these three main characters become connected to one another are largely metaphysical, as is the ultimate scope of Albom's project. Their very different stories, although varying in their thematic and emotional heft, nevertheless all build suspensefully thanks to his technique of interspersing them, revealing each one by bits and pieces.<br /><br />THE TIME KEEPER is a fable of sorts; although readers likely won't feel preached at, they also should come prepared for a story that weaves together religion, philosophy and common sense in a way that results in a very defined message or even moral. The moral, which comes to all three characters in different ways, is certainly one that busy modern readers will be able to get behind. He also adopts a simplified, pared-down prose style to tell his story, making it read --- despite its contemporary setting and relevance --- even more like classic fables that readers may remember from childhood.<br /><br />Albom has gained a well-deserved reputation for writing about matters of faith, mortality and the afterlife in ways that resonate with readers. In THE TIME KEEPER, he does the same thing in a story that also reminds people how to try to live each day.<br /><br />Reviewed by Norah Piehl

B008BDZOZY|4.0|I'm tired of the bland musical product that oozes through corporate pipelines. There's a deadening sameness about the fuzzy guitars and humdrum drumming and affectless vocals of contemporary pop. In this environment, the deliberate creation of an unusual album feels like a gust of fresh air.<br /><br />David Byrne of Talking Heads fame has teamed up with another brilliant nonconformist, St. Vincent (Annie Clark, formerly of Polyphonic Spree), to write and record songs set to danceable electronic beats by John Congleton. They are accompanied by the complex, swooping, angular orchestrations of a very funky horn section. Both artists sing lyrics of ambiguity and anomie that recall some of their intriguing solo performances, but &#34;Love This Giant&#34; doesn't rehash their previous work. It's is a one-of-a-kind hybrid, and I like it a lot.<br /><br />Byrne and Clark open with the first single, &#34;Who,&#34; a very propulsive, rhythmic track on which they duet, but then they start alternating lead vocals. Clark sings the even-numbered and Byrne the odd-numbered songs until they reach &#34;Lazarus,&#34; when they duet again. Clark takes over for the next two tracks, while the final two belong to Byrne.<br /><br />The album has an arc to it, beginning with high-energy music -- jumpy and jagged on the first three cuts (I especially enjoy &#34;Weekend in the Dust&#34;) -- then dialing back the energy somewhat for three songs whose dreamy intros bring to mind minimalist composers like John Adams, Philip Glass, and Steve Reich, particularly on &#34;I Am an Ape.&#34; With &#34;I Should Watch TV,&#34; they leap back for a couple of tracks into the jangly, dissonant sounds that opened the album. When Clark performs &#34;Optimist&#34; and &#34;Lightning&#34; (another favorite of mine), a cool, pensive atmosphere prevails. The penultimate song, &#34;The One Who Broke Your Heart,&#34; has a cheerful, lively tone that blends the music of two more collaborators: the soulful Dap-Kings and the Afro-beat band Antibalas. The last track, &#34;Outside of Space & Time,&#34; is simply a gorgeous love song.<br /><br />There's a lot happening on this recording. It's often sharp and chaotic, yet occasionally smooth and calm, so it really calls for careful, conscious listening. I've heard it all the way through three times now, finding my interest renewed each time. I'm sure to listen several times more to absorb what's going on, which makes this release a clear &#34;buy&#34; for me.<br /><br />A 37-minute live concert of songs from this album is available free online at NPR, together with eight additional minutes of rehearsal footage and insights about the creative process. Filmed at Strathmore Music Center in Rockville, Maryland, it's a must-watch to appreciate the unity of vision with which David Byrne and Annie Clark approach their music and art. The eight-piece brass band is integrated into their choreography, Annie is a badass on guitar, and we get to hear two songs not on the album, St. Vincent's &#34;Marrow&#34; and Talking Heads' &#34;Burning Down the House,&#34; in entirely new settings.balas. Normally I would have run to the library and taken out the book to read, but it was so special to me that I didn't want it to end!<br /><br />The author does a great job with the love affair between Bishop and Clairmont and a great job making history come alive. Her life immediately changes as the manuscript has long been coveted by not only witches, but demons and vampires and until Diana, no one has been able to retrieve the manuscript.  Matthew, among other studies, has been pursuing research and study in genetics. DNA analysis suggests that the other world creatures are dying out.<br /><br />I enjoyed the developing relationship between Matthew and Diana. It was fascinating to read the author's take on what you should feed a vampire that you invite for dinner. So frequently if you come across vampires in reading romance or romantic suspense their personality is developed and some of the more obvious vampire characteristics are touched upon, but the authors don't delve that deeply into their psyches or the problems of living forever. If he's ever in need of money, I know many shady organizations out there would die to hire him for \\"advanced interrogation techniques.\\"<br /><br /> As mentioned so often before, the dual narration provides an interesting double viewpoint from which to watch the story. Still, I applaud Jay Asher's ingenuity and creativity.<br /><br /> This stereoscopic viewpoint looks over no fluff, either.  Asher deals with one of the most serious subjects with respect and tact;  never do we feel that Hannah's tragic decision is taken light of or made fun of.  Thirteen Reasons Why is a book about a grim subject - suicide - and we feel all the gravity of it.  We start from knowing nothing to feeling the weight on Hannah's shoulders.  Events cascade into one another until we see the full picture, guided on by the dual narration.<br /><br /> We're left thinking over all those things we've said or done to someone else that aren't our proudest moments.  And we're driven to become better people, to respect and love one another.<br /> Is Jay Asher's Thirteen Reasons Why great literature?  Yes.  But also, it is a call for everyone, author and editors included, to come together.<br /><br /> Please, to understand one of the greatest tragedies we face, read Thirteen Reasons Why.

B008LZHA3G|5.0|Reading early reviews of \\"Tempest\\", Bob Dylan's 35th studio album, appeared to indicate that this is, perhaps, Dylan's darkest album and one of his strangest.  This is easily the most violent album he's released.  If you look up the word \\"tempest\\" in a dictionary, this is what you will find: \\"a violent commotion, disturbance, or tumult.\\"  This perfectly describes the lyrical content of \\"Tempest\\", and the world that Dylan writes about.  Everything is in an uproar and the end is coming soon. \\"Tempest\\" is the world being torn apart.  The early reviews have been right - this is Dylan's darkest album, and the body count is quite high.  Dylan told Rolling Stone that in the world described in this album, \\"\\"anything goes and you just gotta believe it will make sense.\\"  But in a world being torn asunder, how much will really make sense?\\"<br /><br />\\"Tempest\\" describes a world gone to hell, sometimes literally, and it is up to humanity to learn how to cope with the often senseless brutality inherent in a world such as the one that we live in.  Across the record, you will find Dylan repeatedly angered, fighting mad and ready to take on the world, even when suffering through the pain.  What has been either passed over or only briefly noted about \\"Tempest\\" is its release date - September 11, 2012.  9-11 did turn the world upside down, and releasing an album like \\"Tempest\\" on 9-11 is quite appropriate - globally we have been in a tempest and that date has come to symbolize so much about the uncertainty and brewing storm we find ourselves in<br /><br />Remembering this is an Amazon.com review and this isn't the place to write a historical treatsie, since 9-11-2001, the world has been turned upside down both internationally and for Dylan's homeland, America.  For those who are Americans, we have gotten in two wars, we elected the first black President, and we have seen the worst economic recession since (appropriately enough in the context of Dylan's career) the 1930s and the era of FDR.  At the time of the album's release, a deeply divided America has been desperately trying to crawl out of the recession, with two different political parties offering radically different visions and paths for the nation to trod to a new era of ever hoped for prosperity that seems so far away, with Europe in the middle of economic collapse as well.<br /><br />Dylan told Rolling Stone: \\"\\"I wanted to make something more religious.  I just didn't have enough [religious songs]. Intentionally, specifically religious songs is what I wanted to do. That takes a lot more concentration to pull that off 10 times with the same thread - than it does with a record like I ended up with.\\" Granted, Dylan tells us he wasn't able to pull off the religious album he was wanting to make, but \\"Tempest\\" has quite the religious feel to it . . . provided you are reading the Book of Revelation with a distinctly American historical context informing the events that you see unfolding before you.  The whole album has Old Testament underpinnings of righteous indignation, hell-fire and brimstone, and judgement from the hands of God.<br /><br />Since 1997 with the release of \\"Time Out of Mind\\" and especially since the 2001 album \\"Love and Theft\\", Dylan has focused on pre-rock musical forms.  Dylan is still mining the American songbook in \\"Tempest\\", appropriately enough his most Shakespean in terms of scope and tragedy.  Musically \\"Tempest\\" is squarely in the same tradition as his last four studio albums of original material - folk, jazzy ballads, crooners, bluesy rockers, roadhouse blues, and even some Tex Mex flavor on \\"Early Roman Kings\\" that so characterized \\"Together Through Life\\".  Like the other four albums, musical allusions and direct lifts appear throughout.  \\"Narrow Way\\" uses a chorus from the Mississippi Sheiks' 1934 blues \\"You'll Work Down to Me Someday.\\"  \\"Scarlet Town\\" alludes to poetry written by 19th-century Quaker poet and abolitionist John Greenleaf Whittier.  You'll find echos from Louis Armstrong and the Isley Brothers. I'm sure others will surface as time goes on.<br /><br />Dylan has come into a critical renaissance with \\"Time Out of Mind\\" which has gone unabated since.  While \\"Tempest\\" is Dylan's darkest album, \\"Time Out of Mind\\" is Dylan's saddest, most pain-filled album, even more so than \\"Blood on the Tracks\\". \\"Blood on the Tracks\\" documented Dylan's first divorce - \\"Time Out of Mind\\" documented the pain inherent in the divorce and the aftermath of living for years without his love.  Not that \\"Time Out of Mind\\" is literally about his first divorce, but lyrically though, the pain of lost love, so desperately wanted to be reunited with his lover (whoever she may be), and being left in the wastelands without much hope is ever present on that record.  \\"Time\\" shows the listener Dylan living in the aftermath of the catastrophe that is \\"Blood on the Tracks\\" and the world-worn weariness he feels.  But he left that life with his lady love \\"somewhere back there along the line\\".<br /><br />If \\"Time Out of Mind\\" is Dylan's most vulnerable, a veritable Garden of Gethesame moment for the aging troubador, \\"Tempest\\" is Dylan going out with guns blazing, despite the scars that the sun didn't heal.  He still feels the pain of lost love, still feels the inherent sadness and strangeness of living in a brutal, decadent world full of senseless violence, but regardless, he will press on.  And that truly is the strength of \\"Tempest\\".  Despite the darkness and the anger, there are some real humanizing moments.  The fourteen minute title track both sensitively handles the Titantic tragedy and also acknowledges the pain and the confusion those who were left must have felt with their families and loved ones dead.<br /><br />Despite all the rage, the fear, the death, the murder, Dylan ends the album on a distinctly humanizing, empathetic note: he pays tribute to a fallen hero of so many, John Lennon . to the slain Beatle John Lennon, who Mark David Chapmanmurdered outside the Dakota apartment building in New York City on December 8th, 1980.  Like all the other songs, \\"Roll On John\\" is violent, but of all the songs here, this is the most elegant and thoughtful meditation to what it means to have to carry on when someone you have loved and admired was taken away so forcibly and so prematurely, and the love Dylan feels for Lennon is quite evident.  And really, that's the heart of the album.  Like \\"Tempest\\" says, sometimes there's no understanding \\"judgement of God\\".  But we have to press on anyway.<br /><br />We live in a strange, sad world, marked with violence, radical terroists, economic uncertainy, nations marked by war and starvation, and power hungry leaders bent on domination.  Dylan provides a snapshot of that world, and shows us how he has dealt with [in] it.  In that regard, \\"Tempest\\" is one of the timeliest records Dylan has ever made.<br /><br />***A brief note about 9-11:  There are two notable facts about this date: first, it is eleven years to the day after the attack on the World Trade Center.  Second, September 11, 2001 was the day that Dylan released his acclaimed masterpiece, \\"Love and Theft\\".  Like the JFK murder in Dallas for those alive during that time, people can tell you where they were on 9-11.  Myself?  Between 8:00 to 8:45AM, I was on Amazon.com on 9-11, posting my review to \\"Love and Theft\\" from an earlier preview copy that I had gotten several days before.  The review is still up, and it's the second oldest review for that album currently available on Amazon.

0224097385|5.0|Ian  McEwan remains one of my favorite living authors. His latest novel SWEET TOOTH  makes my affection for him even deeper. This is another of those stories like Kazuo Ishiguro's  NEVER LET ME GO that too much said about the plot ruins the novel for other readers. I will quote, however, from the first paragraph  of the novel as the narrator herself gives  some secrets-- the book is chockfull of them and irony runs rampant-- away right away: \\"My name is Serena Frome (rhymes with plume) and almost 40 years ago [the early 1970's] I was sent on a secret mission for the British security service. I didn't return safely. Within eighteen months of joining I was sacked, having disgraced myself and ruined my lover, though he certainly had a hand in his own undoing.\\" In the next 300 plus pages Mr. McEwan in flawless prose fills the reader in on how these events came about. The last 20 or so of those pages is the kind that you read through while time stands still. I reacted the same to the last pages of  a novel by another favorite writer many years ago, SOPHIE'S CHOICE by William Styron, one of the supreme joys of reading fine novels.<br /><br />Serena, as complex a character as any of Henry James` women characters, is forced by her mother to study math at Cambridge while her real love is literature, specifically fiction. \\"My mother told me she would never forgive me and she would never forgive herself  if I went off to read English and became no more than a slightly better housewife than she was.\\"  She also is quite adept at getting into relationships  that have no future although she soldiers on in her story  that is set right in the middle of British politics in the early 1970's.  (Serena votes for Wilson for prime minister.) One of the things I find so intriguing about  this novel is all the references made to writers. There are literally dozens of them: Kingsley Amis, Angus Wilson, Edward Thomas, W. H. Auden, Arthur Miller, Bertrand Russell, Margaret Drabble, Iris Murdoch, Robert Lowell, T. S. Eliot et al. Martin Amis, a friend of the writer, also makes a brief appearance. And the characters Tom Haley and Ian Hamilton have a conversation about the poem \\"Whitsun Weddings\\" by Philip Larkin similar to the one that McEwan had with his good friend Christopher Hitchens just days before Hitchens'  death  and that Mr. McEwan recounts in perhaps the most moving of many eulogies given for him. (He also dedicates this novel to Mr. Hitchens.)  I defy you to read McEwan's  words on Hitchens' final days without your eyes burning. In addition to all the other  things going on in this novel , in a strange way it is a book about  books. It is also about deceit, betrayal, jealousy,  innocence (maybe inexperience in the ways of the world is a better word), similarities of  the devices opposing governments use to accomplish similar goals, and ultimately about  forgiveness and four letter words--both hope and love.<br /><br />While Mr. McEwan is a master of creating complex main characters, he is  as brilliant with minor characters as well.  Serena's father, the Anglican bishop who \\"dabbled routinely in the supernatural\\" and won't let his daughters smoke in the house is a prime example. There is a particularly poignant scene late in the novel where, with just a few paragraphs on the part of the author,  we get a completely  illuminating picture of this character.  Serena goes home for a visit at Christmas and begins weeping when her father meets her at the door and folds her into his arms. \\"This was the copious hopeless sort of crying you might hear from a tired child. . . Instantly,  I felt his body tense, even though he continued to hold me.\\" Her father asks if he could \\"fetch\\" her mother. \\"I thought I knew what he was thinking -- that now it was the turn of his older daughter to be pregnant or lost to some other modern disaster, and that whatever womanly mess was now soaking this freshly ironed purple shirt was better managed by a woman.\\"  I have read practically all of Mr.  McEwan's novels and cannot recall one I liked  better.ad practically all of Mr.  McEwan's novels and cannot recall one I liked  better.

B008LZHA3G|5.0|Free online premiers, advance videos, selected songs streamed to mobile devices in ten different countries- no album by Bob Dylan has ever been as highly anticipated as \\"Tempest\\". Already the internet has posts claiming it's not as good as \\"Blond On Blond\\" or \\"Highway 61 Revisited\\". The way to appreciate \\"Tempest\\" is to enjoy it for what it is NOT.<br /><br />For fifty years, Dylan has not only put his indelible stamp on popular music, he has done more to change it than any other single artist. He once wrote \\"Tin Pan Alley is dead. I put an end to it\\". This is not braggadocio, merely fact. Today, Dylan creates songs for a new era. Granted, he still utilizes the blues, folk and early jazz sounds on his later recordings, but he has no interest in revisiting the past. \\"Tempest\\" is not \\"Desire\\" or \\"Oh Mercy\\". What you hear is what you get.<br /><br />\\"Duquesne Whistle\\" starts the disc off quietly with Donnie Herron's steel guitar and Dylan's piano playing a relaxed melody until drummer George Receli kicks the song into chugging Western swing. Robert Hunter wrote the lyrics to this track. \\"Soon After Midnight\\" is a lovely ballad with typical Dylan wit: \\"I'm searching for phrases to sing your praises\\", but there is darkness here- \\"I've been down on the killing floors/ I've faced stronger walls than yours\\".<br /><br />\\"Long and Wasted Years\\" and \\"Pay In Blood\\" are my favorite cuts. \\"Years\\" is Dylan in a very direct conversation with a woman who probably wants to run as she listens to him ramble negatively about everything around him. Another great lyric: \\"I wear dark glasses to cover my eyes/ there's secrets in them I can't disguise\\". Guitarist Charlie Sexton plays a catchy repeating descending scale that will stay in your head long after you're done listening. \\"Pay in Blood\\" is the hardest song Dylan has written in years. Whoever pushed him into this much anger is responsible for some caustic writing: \\"You bastard, I'm supposed to respect you? Hear me holler, hear me moan. I pay in blood, but not my own\\".<br /><br />\\"Early Roman Kings\\" is the type of Muddy Waters style blues that Dylan has excelled at over the years. David Hidalgo of Los Lobos adds accordion to this track giving the song a Tex/ Mex feel that was featured on \\"Together Through Life\\". Again, Receli's drumming is highlighted, as is long time bassist Tony Garnier. On more than half the disc, Garnier plays stand up bass which fits the dark mood of the album perfectly. \\"Tin Angel\\" may be the most foreboding track on the album. This minor key tune reminds me of Dylan's \\"Tweeter and the Monkey Man\\" by the Traveling Wilburys. Garnier's upright is paired with some tasty banjo by Herron.<br /><br />The title song is the most heralded one on the disc. Dealing with the sinking of the Titanic one hundred years ago, the track has forty seven verses and features violin from both Herron and Hidalgo. Although the song has no chorus, it never gets boring or repetitious (hey, it worked for the Edmund Fitzgerald). Dylan wryly name checks \\"Leo\\" in the verses, probably as an homage to Leo Di Caprio for quoting him in the movie.<br /><br />\\"Roll On John\\" is Dylan's tribute to one of his great friends, the late John Lennon. This isn't one of his greatest compositions, but the feeling of longing is genuine. The track has a beautiful acoustic guitar intro (think \\"You're A Big Girl Now\\" from \\"Blood On The Tracks\\").<br /><br />Some of Dylan's fans aren't comfortable with the coarseness of his voice in recent years. I believe his voice fits his songs over the years to accommodate his writing, especially since 1997's \\"Time Out Of Mind\\". Marianne Faithfull experienced the same change in her voice after years of hard living roughed up her vocals, but gave her singing a compelling edge only earned over time. For these singers, it isn't always pretty, but it WORKS.<br /><br />There are no weak tracks on this disc. No, it isn't \\"Bringing It All Back Home\\" or \\"Desire\\"- nor should it be. Dylan is makingmusic different from his past glories- but it is just as valid.

B0061JPYX2|5.0|First off, we've got to give producers Rami and Ronson credit for giving this album the incredible continuity it has. Considering they had to pick from left over odds and ends, everything flows together uniformly... even Bennett's duet doesn't sound out of place here - but I'm getting ahead of myself.<br /><br />Kicking things off, the reggie-tinged cover of OUR DAY WILL COME was an inspired choice on Amy's part. You wouldn't want her to go down the remake road too often, but this is a perfect pick, and her leisure, care-free vocals give this classic just the right retro-sheen...<br />If she'd have been around, she might have nixed BETWEEN THE CHEATS from seeing the light of day, or at least re-recorded the vocals (you almost wonder if this was a scratch track and it was all Rami had to work with). However, SO wonderfully outrageous and over the top, it can almost be viewed as a DooWop song, or a pariody of a DooWop song.<br />The alternate version of TEARS DRY ON THEIR OWN is an interesting listen, but you understand one time through it why the one that graces B2B won the day.<br />In the case of WILL YOU STILL LOVE ME TOMORROW, Ace producer Ronson makes his first misstep; The ponderous, flatfooted (someone else in these reviews said \\"hamfisted\\") production really weighs down Amy's otherwise buoyant vocals. You can feel he was probably going for that Phil Specter \\"Wall of Sound\\" thing, but it just falls flat.<br />That being said, he quickly redeems himself with the infectious fun of the upbeat rocker VALERIE;<br />And then there's THE GIRL FROM IPENIMA... I NEVER thought I'd EVER want to hear this by anyone other than Getz/Giberto, but Amy's joyous vocals breath new life into this time-defying masterpiece; Her inspired scatt singing bursts fourth like a bird taking flight, and you can surely feel that this was from a time in her life when her future seemed bright, and the world spread out unbounded before her.<br />Shifing gears, the more contemplative and introspective HALFTIME could easily be the Jewel in this album's Crown. Along with the previously mentioned ODWC, this one really feels like it COULD have been part of the true 3rd LP. Amy's quiet, slightly halting vocals here give way to the alternate version of WAKE UP ALONE, with it's haunting, sun-too-bright-in-the-morning feel. Although we can guess that the version of WUA that graced B2B was probably picked for that album's overall pacing, it must have been a hard choice between the two of these.<br />Back to a fun reality, the love/hate frienemeny atmosphere of BEST FRIENDS, RIGHT? can't help but make you smile. This segways into the beautiful, smoke-curling languadness of the Bennett duet BODY AND SOUL. As mentioned earlier, there's no jarring continuity issues here with this and anything we've heard up to this point, and the two's harmonizing is amazing. Keen ears will detect, though, that Amy's voice, while still defiant, was fast becoming a battered instrument. Setting the stage for...<br />...The chilling, devestating coda of A SONG FOR YOU. This is like being thrown into ice water. Although her singing is slurry and her words almost unintelligable, the overall impact of this tone poem cannot be missed... This is Amy singing to us from the grave, and the production carries with it the cautionary tale of art, excess, addiction and early demise... Accentuated all the more by Amy's final, spoken musings on Donny Hathaway an Marvin Gaye.<br />Listening to this last piece in the dark truly gave me a start the first time I heard it. I laid there wondering what else she was going to say... But after that, silence...

B005LAII3U|2.0|The stories and novels of sci-fi futurist Philip K. Dick have been grist for the movie mill for quite some time since BLADE RUNNER hit the big screen in 1982, but it took until 1990 to get a new story of his onto the screen, based on the short story \\"We Can Remember It For You Wholesale\\", and that film then became Paul Verhoeven's over-the-top violent sci-fi extravaganza TOTAL RECALL, starring Arnold Schwarzenegger, Sharon Stone, Ronny Cox and Rachel Ticotin. Aside from it totally ignoring most of Dick's story, it's an incredibly fun, campy and action-packed romp.<br /><br />With remakes usually equaling big money for the studios (especially if they can also squeeze out some 3D post-conversions or IMAX formatting), and the technical advances made over the last 22 years, director Len Wiseman (the first two UNDERWORLD films, LIVE FREE OR DIE HARD) took the reins of a not-totally-a-remake and not-totally-a-reimagining of TOTAL RECALL. As is Wiseman's forte, he makes it slick and gorgeous with some great effects and well-choreographed action sequences. Sadly, he also forgot to add any fun to this tale and he didn't allow any of the actors, such as Colin Farrell, Kate Beckinsale, Bryan Cranston and Jessica Biel, to develop any chemistry with each other.<br /><br />Much of the original story's elements are still gone, but Wiseman and screenwriters Kurt Wimmer (also the writer/director of the great EQUILIBRIUM) and Mark Bomback (who also wrote LIVE FREE OR DIE HARD as well as... RACE TO WITCH MOUNTAIN...) as well as master production designer Patrick Tatopolous (who, along with working with Wiseman on UNDERWORLD: EVOLUTION and LIVE FREE OR DIE HARD, directed the third UNDERWORLD installment, subtitled RISE OF THE LYCANS) have firmly planted this film in a much grittier world of the future than was seen in the original film. It's the late 21st century, and much of the world has been made uninhabitable by chemical warfare, so all of the remaining humanity is now divided into two places in the world to live: the more well-to-do live in The United Federation of Britian and the more disenfranchised live on the other side of the world in the remains of Austrailia, now simply known as The Colony, and the only travel from one side to the other is via a massive gravity elevator called The Fall. We follow the humdrum life of Douglas Quaid (Farrell, playing it totally straight) who is a skilled laborer and his wife Lori (played with relish by Beckinsale). They live together in The Colony, which is a hybrid of the poorest parts of many different countries, with the two main influences being Rio de Janeiro and BLADE RUNNER's Los Angeles (the stacked-level apartments; brightly colored and seedy areas). He dreams of a life he never had and looks for some escapism, and this leads him to Rekall, a memory-implantation parlor where it gives you memories that feel like the real thing, and because his actual dreams are that of a tough-guy black-ops type spy, he selects to get implanted with their \\"Secret Agent\\" package. Although, this touches off a brutal assault on the parlor itself by a number of Federal Police, who Quaid suddenly finds that he is able to calmly disarm and kill all of them. He heads back home to Lori, who initially plays the loving wife and then turns murderous (and British) as she tries to kill him. After disarming her, she tells him that he is not Douglas Quaid; he's got false memories implanted in his head. This catalyst sets off a chain of events where Quaid becomes involved with the mysterious \\"girl of his dreams\\" Melina (Biel, again, totally straight), the corrupt Chancellor Cohaagen (Bryan Cranston, in a pretty physical performance) and the resistance fighting against Cohaagen's plans to take over the world, led my Matthias (Bill Nighy, not British for some reason). Also, just in case you didn't know, this could still just be a dream.<br /><br />If you've seen the original, you literally have no reason to see this film, becausealthough the film does change the plot of going to Mars and the mutants and the big \\"Blue Sky on Mars\\" mission, this film plays out very much in the same way. The only moments of \\"humor\\" to be found in this film are little nudge-nudge wink-winks to the original (the inexplicable appearence of a three-breasted woman, the fat red-headed woman going through security saying \\"Two weeks\\", etc.). Aside from that, the only fun to be found in this film are the masterfully-created action sequences that take place somewhat early on in the film, such as the fight between Doug and Lori, the magnetic car chase and the super-duper elevator shaft sequence are all incredibly well done and look about as polished as anything in modern film. Unfortunately, the cast (with the exception of Beckinsale) play these moments with deadly seriousness, and it's a real detriment to the enjoyment of the film. This is a SUMMER ACTION FILM REMAKE OF A SUMMER ACTION FILM. Why everyone decided that this should be played in the most serious tone possible was a major misstep for everyone involved. I'm certainly not in favor of all the summer films being without substance or story and should only be these magical one-liner machines, but not only is this film bereft of any real substance, it also just seems like everyone's going through the motions of making futuristic sci-fi. The only one that seems like they're enjoying themselves is Beckinsale, but of course being the director's wife does have its perks. And to be fair to Beckinsale, she is really good.<br /><br />Also other unfortunate side-effects of this remake/reimagining is the distinct lack of two things that were so essential to the success of the first film: First is the violence. This is an exceedingly sterile film. Most of the enemies that appear are these synthetic drone robots which, while being interesting, are by definition soulless automatons. They decided very early on that this was going to be somewhat family-friendly (MUCH more so than the original at least), and made it as bloodless and antiseptic as possible, and the ultra-futuristic setting of the film (people using canoes to navigate flooded streets, elevators that spring up and down and left and right like a bizarre level of a video game, and the deus ex machina of The Fall) is all slicker and polished than it should be. Second, and much more importantly, is the distinction between fantasy and reality, which is a major theme not only of this film, but of Dick's body of work in general. Oddly enough, this was something that was taken much more seriously and more efficiently dealt with in the original film, and it's something that this version tries to do justice, but aside from a few moments in a overly dramatic scene with Farrell, Biel, and Farrell's work buddy Harry (played by Bokeem Woodbine), it doesn't quite connect.<br /><br />As far as performances other than Beckinsale's, whose virtues are dubious but evident, Cranston decides that rather than play a more deliciously \\"evil\\" villain as Cox did so brilliantly in the original, he plays it straight which is to his strengths as we are aware of him currenly as the brilliant actor that gives Walter White such vivid life, but also have forgotten about the time he spent as the bizarre father on MALCOLM IN THE MIDDLE. This is an actor who is not only dramatic but incredibly comic and both strengths could have been played to. Farrell, who has shown incredible range in his roles over the years, plays this very sullen. There is none of the glint in his eye that he showed in pretty much all of his other roles and none of the charm that one needs to carry a big action film. Jessica Biel is still as bland as she has ever been. I've really tried to find roles she's had over the years that can convince me that she has something to bring to the table other than just a sexy body, and it still hasn't happened, and that's also a big reason that there is zero chemistry between the two leads. Bill Nighy isjust wasted here.<br /><br />I know that this is a film that should exist on its own merits, rather than being compared to the original, but that's part of the territory when it comes to a remake; it's either better, worse or on the same level, and despite the lack of anything close to subtlety in Verhoeven's original, that film is eminently watchable and still very lovable for all of its action cliches. Wiseman's version of TOTAL RECALL is only remarkable for its outstanding visual design (which would have worked much better in any other futuristic action film) and its well-orchestrated set pieces, but sadly lacks the spectacle, heart or fun that a big-budget actioner should have.<br /><br />2/5 Starsn the original at least), and made it as bloodless and antiseptic as possible, and the ultra-futuristic setting of the film (people using canoes to navigate flooded streets, elevators that spring up and down and left and right like a bizarre level of a video game, and the deus ex machina of The Fall) is all slicker and polished than it should be. Wiseman's version of TOTAL RECALL is only remarkable for its outstanding visual design (which would have worked much better in any other futuristic action film) and its well-orchestrated set pieces, but sadly lacks the spectacle, heart or fun that a big-budget actioner should have.<br /><br />2/5 Stars

B008LZHA3G|4.0|There's been no singer-songwriter in the near 60-year history of rock music who's a better storyteller than Bob Dylan. He proved it with his (now legendary) humble folk beginnings, and he continues to prove it today, as evidenced by the very dark, very epic 'Tempest,' an album that can proudly stand next to the best of his post-'Blood on the Tracks' repertoire.<br /><br />After a somewhat lean 1980's and even leaner early-mid 90's as far as quality output from Dylan, he's now in the midst of a pretty remarkable 15-year renaissance beginning with 1997's near-perfect 'Time Out of Mind.' Every album in that span has been extremely well-received and critically-acclaimed--with only '09's 'Together Through Life' receiving even the mildest of criticisms--and I have a pretty strong feeling this trend will continue with his latest. As someone who's been slightly burnt-out on Dylan over the past few years, I think it's safe to say he won't be coming out of my rotation anytime in the foreseeable future.<br /><br />Right out of the gate his larynx sounds utterly destroyed on the swingin,' country-jukebox dance-floor number, \\"Duquesne Whistle,\\" but that doesn't take anything away from the song. In fact, it adds a certain kind of charm, giving Dylan a swagger that's been wholly earned through his 50 years of musical storytelling and lessons. His voice is one that demands to be listened to--now as much as ever. And I dare you to stay still in your seat during this track.<br /><br />After bringing it down a notch with the tender ballad, \\"Soon After Midnight,\\" Dylan kicks it back into first with the rowdy Chicago blues stomp of \\"Narrow Way,\\" and when his weary, gravelly voice spouts \\"It's a long road, it's a long and narrow way,\\" you believe him wholeheartedly. And later, when he sings \\"I pay in blood, but not my own\\" on \\"Pay in Blood,\\" you almost wish you didn't believe, so convincing are his words. \\"Scarlet Town\\" is one of Dylan's most haunting, most beautifully hypnotic songs ofhis career, and even at seven-plus minutes, it feels too short.<br /><br />\\"Early Roman Kings\\" is another blues number, copping the oft-used and instantly recognizable \\"Mannish Boy\\" riff that Muddy Waters made famous six decades ago, and to good effect, but the centerpiece of the album is undoubtedly the 14-minute title track, a 45-verse waltz recounting, in Dylan's inimitable way, the Titanic disaster of 1912. He does justice to the victims of that tragedy, putting his heart and soul into each and every verse, even while slyly dropping \\"Leo\\" and \\"Rose\\" into the song, obvious references to James Cameron's 1997 blockbuster. The first time I heard it, I thought it ran a bit long, and had to control my urge to skip ahead. The next time I just drifted off and let Dylan bring me into his world, into HIS version of the event, and the result was powerful--even moving.<br /><br />The album, while mostly filled with revenge and redemption-themed songs, closes on a much prettier note with \\"Roll On John,\\" a loving tribute to John Lennon. Being the last of three straight epic, somewhat meandering tracks, one would think the listener would grow weary by this point, but that could not be further from the truth. This was another one that had to grow on me--as it initially seemed directionless and weirdly sung--but I soon viewed it to be one of the most beautiful, touching moments of Dylan's entire oeuvre, even quoting Lennon himself with \\"I heard the news today, oh boy,\\" referring to his 1980 assassination.<br /><br />It's a fitting, perfect end to what could be argued is his most impressive and consistent effort since 2001's 'Love and Theft,' possibly even since 'Time Out of Mind' from four years prior, though not quite on the level of that, or his 60's and 70's classics, imo. But that would be asking too much. There aren't many artists who've remained as relevant as he has for fifty years and thirty-five albums, while at the same time continually putting out such strikingly original work.<br /><br />My hope is that he'll continue to do so for another decade, at least. But I count myself--and the world at large--as fortunate to have had him around for any length of time at all.<br /><br />4.5 out of 5

0547564651|3.0|(NOTE:  I was provided with a free ARC of this book prior to this review.)<br /><br />Since my daughter entered an urban school district with many failing schools, I've been interested in how you can bring the whole system up given the challenges faced with poverty, lack of parental involvement, and the stress of living in high crime areas.<br /><br />Author Paul Tough takes a look at the approaches being taken to help high risk students and comes away with the hypothesis that what helps kids at risk succeed is the following:<br /><br />1) A life in which a child is shielded from stress as much as possible, both at home and in the surrounding environment.<br />2) Building qualities of character like grit, resilience, and optimism.<br />3) Community, school, and even medical programs that focus on providing support for these students and drive them to succeed rather than warehouse them until they reach the age where they're forced to fend for themselves.<br /><br />Unfortunately, How Children Succeed spends so much time pointing to anecdotes that support these principles that the science and statistics that support it tend to get lost in the shuffle.  While these ideas seem intuitively obvious, it would have been nice to read more about contrasting approaches to helping kids at risk and why those approaches may be failing.<br /><br />One thing that the book makes clear is that as a country we seem to have spent precious little time analyzing what works in education and instead are focused on the next big thing in solving what ails many of our public schools.  Paul Tough also calls both liberals and conservatives to task for politicizing education without addressing that the arguments of both sides have merit.  Yes, conservatives are right when they say that kids can't succeed without building their character, and liberals who have difficulty discussing the role that bad parenting has in the failure of kids to achieve are not helping.  But Tough also says that liberals are right in saying it takes a village to help these kids succeed, and that we need to invest money in programs to help provide the support they need.<br /><br />How Children Succeed provides an interesting glimpse into the ways in which we can help our children and our public schools perform better.  If nothing else, it makes it clear that there is no one miracle solution that will solve our problems in public education.s are right in saying it takes a village to help these kids succeed, and that we need to invest money in programs to help provide the support they need.<br /><br />How Children Succeed provides an interesting glimpse into the ways in which we can help our children and our public schools perform better.  If nothing else, it makes it clear that there is no one miracle solution that will solve our problems in public education.

044024630X|4.0|This story is set in 1997 and it takes us back to a younger Jack Reacher who is still with the military. When a dead body turns up pointing fingers to a soldier from a nearby military base, Jack Reacher is sent undercover to a small town in Mississippi. At the same time another MP is officially sent to the base to investigate the case. Reacher is wary from the beginning and his wariness is not without reason. In this small sleepy town not everything as it seems. There has been more than one murder, soldiers on the base may be involved in military activities that are not publicly known and the local Sheriff may not be what she claims to be either...<br /><br />Jack Reacher is a class act and he is the only character with the `larger than life' feel that I believe in. The six feet five inches tall, weighing between a hundred and hundred fifty pounds - Jack Reacher is a MP who can not only take out several opponents singlehandedly, but also has excellent deduction skills. Once again his power of deduction, physical strength and alertness is put to test. There is this particular situation where Reacher inspects a destroyed car for clues that is absolutely amazing. Also, for the first time, I felt that there was a character who could truly match Reacher in his skills, strengths and thirst for justice. Elizabeth Deveraux is that character. She is almost a female version of Jack Reacher himself. She is an ex-marine who has already put in a good number of years in service and thus has the experience and knowledge herself. She is `finds out' Reacher immediately and though they do not trust each other at the beginning, as they work together they do develop a mutual respect.<br /><br />As a plot, it is the traditional `who-dun-it' sort. As the story progresses more than one suspect are revealed so that the readers are bound to play the guessing game. But yes, it is not as complex as some other Reacher novels. A few familiar names/faces turned up in this book including Francis Neagley - who I have a soft spot for. This book also tells us how and why Reacher left the military and became a `drifter'. Without giving out any obvious spoilers - during the course of the plot, situations arise when he questions the military policies and has conflicts with his superiors. But ultimately his adherence to truth makes him take some truly drastic measures.<br /><br />If you have read a Jack Reacher novel before, then you always pick up the latest book with certain expectations. With Lee Child's consistency, with each novel your level of expectation rises higher and higher. So when I picked up this 16th novel in the Jack Reacher Series (this must be my 11th or 12th time reading a  Jack Reacher), I picked it up with much trepidation. Yet under the normal \\"bell\\" distribution this is mathematically impossible. RIP CAPM.

B008Z9YLV2|4.0|Whenever a book gets adapted to a movie, something is lost.  Whether that's character depth, minor plot elements, or perspective, it's simply not possible to fully translate what is essentially an inner, thought-driven medium into something we experience through more external senses.  In the case of The Hunger Games, the challenge is to translate a first-person-narrated book into more of a third-person experience.  How can the directors express Katniss' thoughts when she only speaks to other people?  How does she really feel about Gale?  About Peeta?  It's tricky, but the directors use understatement very well (witness the \\"You call that a kiss?\\" note) and express Katniss' emotions through other people's lines (the movie gives words to the Hunger Games commentators; the book does not).  Short shrift is given to Katniss' relationship with her mother, with Prim, with other people in her district, and even to some degree with Gale.  Peeta's parents are barely mentioned; Katniss' father never is.  Probably necessary casualties of a film adaptation, so read the book for a bit more character development.<br /><br />I'll say that the movie was far less gory than I expected, having read the books.  That's a good thing, given the movie's target audience.  No serious blood, but the neck-breaking scene was still disturbing, and Clove is still her usual sadistic self.  The muttations are far less demonic than in the book, and Peeta's injury appears somewhat trivial here compared to the book's festering wound right by his crotch.  No tourniquets here, no post-Games heart attacks for Peeta, no false leg. Still, don't let your children see this without seeing it yourself--I've seen tons of R-rated films that are far less disturbing (though may use more swearing).  They (and you) will want to talk about it.<br /><br />The Hunger Games tackles themes (children killing each other, authoritarian government) most of us would never think about on our own; for that alone, it's worth experiencing either as a book or as a movie.
B007IXWKUK|4.0|Having read Book 1, it was a \\"must do\\" to read Book 2. I enjoyed the book, and there was enough \\"suspense\\" that made it a real page turner. He is ninja-like in his dedication to the art of sushi-making and considers it more of a lifestyle, a mindset really, than he does a job. If you happen to make it to Japan, the minimum $375 and one month advanced reservation it takes to get into Jiro's sushi restaurant would absolutely be worth it...if nothing else but for the experience alone.<br /><br />It's rare to find a film that so perfectly compliments its subject matter with its camerawork and sequencing. Please spend the few bucks to check out this rental--you can gain a new life perspective for $3, which is about as cheap as they come.

And, one time, you may get two, then one. So in my little metaphor, those sprinkles are Coelho's simplest ideas. Sometimes they go right over your head and then, when you re-read the book - bang! - the ideas just snap and you get it. And Coelho has just lightly dusted the icing, but, I tell you, those sprinkles are delicious; the ideas just wonderful.<br /><br />The book's \\"icing\\" is the story's symbolism. Someone else may call it a thick layer of unrealistic goodness, something to be expected in a fable, but I just call it \\"symbolism\\". There is so much in this book to catch on to that sometimes, you just can't keep up. But, re-reading will fix that.<br /><br />And then, in the middle, there is the filling (of the cupcake), just a bit. That is the truth that is to be found in the main character's journey, the truth to be found in all the lessons and morals, all of it.<br /><br />Sometimes, though, you don't want to eat the cupcake.
