B007XU0N1G|5.0|Is this a Rhapsody album or a Luca Turilli album? These two names have become so synonymous over the years as to be virtually inseparable. Even during Luca's solo career I wondered why he decided to strike out on his own since his first two albums could easily have been placed under the Rhapsody catalog, both musically and lyrically. His last solo album, however, was a drastic change that would not have fit so comfortably and now that Luca has split with the \\"of Fire\\" crew that direction has come back with a vengeance.<br /><br />This new Rhapsody is truly a force to be reckoned with, and just a few listens to this masterpiece will surely convince anyone that this iteration is the real deal. To be fair, I shouldn't dismiss Staropoli and crew since they have yet to put forth their new album and I am convinced those guys are competent at writing amazing music as well, but let me just say that Luca has set bar really high with Ascending to Infinity.<br /><br />The new singer, Alessandro Conti, is a far cry from Fabio Lione, as Conti is a schooled tenor and shows off his impressive range very frequently. Far from showboating, Conti is literally a breath of fresh air, combining a somewhat typical but very well done register of power metal singing ala Michael Kiske with his tenor background, and quite frequently under the same breath, which is a really unique dichotomy that I can't recall hearing in any other band. Conti also visits a mid range regularly, which is lower than his main voice, reminding me of a less cheesy and much manlier Rob Rock and is exemplified superbly in Dark Fate of Atlantis. This lower register is actually quite soothing on the ears, giving off an operatic feel and making the transition into his tenor range more sinuous. To be honest, I can't recall being this impressed by a power metal singer since Oliver Hartmann and Matthias Blad, and it's this versatility that has helped Luca explore the creative freedom he desired.<br /><br />Indeed, one could almost say that Luca was held back by the \\"other\\" Rhapsody. Lione, though very good, does not have the same range as Conti, and the fantasy themes, as Luca has expressly stated, did not match the lyrical freedom he desired (even if it seemed Luca ironically held himself back when he went solo, writing fantasy based lyrics as well). One doesn't have to go far into the album to make this realization, either; the first full track, which happens to be the title track, is one of Rhapsody's best songs to date, boasting enthusiastic choruses and a soaring performance by Conti who shows off his vocal mettle with tremendous gusto. Impressive is certainly an understatement, and it foreshadows the grandiose ideas jammed onto this single disc. At no moment does the music ever sound cluttered or pretentious; Luca's experience has been distilled over the years, his creativity liberated yet directed clearly and eloquently. This is indeed the work of a master artist who knows exactly which strokes to paint and colors to use.<br /><br />While the title track, Dark Fate of Atlantis, and Clash of the Titans are more traditional Rhapsody songs in terms of composition and layout, other songs such as Tormento e Passione are a bit more divergent, taking on a theatric quality, complete with two characters exchanging dialogue, a strong emphasis on Conti's tenor range, and lyrics written entirely in Italian. Its placement smack dab in the middle of the track order implies an \\"intermission\\" of sorts, to shake things up, as well as the interesting cover of Alessandro Safina's Luna, which I would've preferred at the end of the album, actually, since that is technically a \\"popera\\" song and breaks the momentum just ever so slightly, despite Rhapsody's arrangement being more metal.<br /><br />Another surprise is the relative lack of spoken words, or within the context of a Rhapsody album, narrative. Christopher Lee may have done a good job on past albums but I've never been a fan of two to threeminute narratives before the final track or 45-60 seconds of talking in the middle of a track. As important to the story as it may be, I find it breaks the momentum and cheapens the album, but FINALLY Luca has found a happy medium. His new narrator doesn't even get a cumulative minute of air time, the longest span occurring on (you guessed it) the last track and even then it's a mere 30 seconds in length. And the good news is that he doesn't even sound that bad; this time it's the somewhat cheesy dialogue that stands out. Indeed, Luca has apparently forgotten what fillers are and written an album that is 100% awesome 100% of the time.<br /><br />Up until now Dawn of Victory had been my favorite album from Rhapsody but Ascending to Infinity surpasses it easily. It combines the bombast and orchestral emphasis of earlier albums with the aggression of Power of the Dragonflame, and magnifies everything tenfold. True, it may still bear Luca's stylistic leaning and in that sense there hasn't been a tremendous amount of forward movement, but that doesn't mean the music hasn't improved or is no longer relevant. Luca's trademark style is merely the backbone. It's how he evolves that style to fit changing tastes and influences that matters, incorporating new themes and musical elements that were previously unfeasible and at the same time staying true to himself and to his fans. This is the ultimate Rhapsody album and this is only the beginning.minute narratives before the final track or 45-60 seconds of talking in the middle of a track. Indeed, Luca has apparently forgotten what fillers are and written an album that is 100% awesome 100% of the time.<br /><br />Up until now Dawn of Victory had been my favorite album from Rhapsody but Ascending to Infinity surpasses it easily. 0061122416|3.0|The Alchemist by Paulo Coelho is a slow novel that is a collision of ideas creating a confusing yet inspiring story which forces the reader to take one sentence at a time. The story had many lessons to be learned which addresses general people, but rereading these lessons to fully grasp their meaning is a common habit from page one. Though the story lacked excitement, I found a certain entertainment when I was able to reflect on my own life when deeper meanings were revealed. The saying I got the most personal thought out of was right towards the start of the novel. Coelho said \\"Everyone seems to have a clear idea of how other people should live their lives, but none about his or her own.\\" Before this was said, I wanted to give up reading out of boredom but when I began to learn how to find the lessons meant to be learned, I enjoyed the book that much more.<br /><br />  The hardest thing about this story for me though, was that so many conflicting ideas were put together. The combination of multiple religions was most prevalent. There were references from the Bible and about God, but then it would shift into another religion such as Muslim by referring to omens from Allah. I didn't particularly like that every religion became one because it made the story that much harder to follow.<br /><br />  This story started out as realistic novel until the end when the impossible idea of turning yourself into the wind became possible which continued to add to my list of colliding ideas. The story should have all been realistic or not because it would have been a nice constant element.<br /><br />  Overall The Alchemist is a novel you would want to read if you needed life inspiration but don't mind feeling a little lost along the way. That's how life is though; you learn and grow on the journey but getting to your destination can be messy. The destination doesn't always matter either and that's what the book really teaches. Though the story was confusing, in the aspect of important lessons learned, The Alchemist was worth reading.

0061122416|3.0|The book The Alchemist, by Paulo Coelho, might just hold the key to the secret of happiness. If entered with an open mind and a willing to look deeper, it provides the reader with a huge sense of enlightenment and refreshment, as if opening a door to the deeper side of the universe. It uses many references to all kinds of religion, which gives those religious more hope, insight, and understanding of the world around them. However, the book still leaves even non-religious people with a sense of purpose for their time here on earth. As Socrates once said, \\"The unexamined life is not worth living.\\"  This book puts you in the middle of the path leading to an \\"examined life.\\" It makes you ponder your purpose, dreams and placement through a story of a shepherd boy who achieves what seems impossible. As he embarks along his journey in search of his dreams, you find yourself inspired to search out the same in your own life and chase it. Though the events in this story may seem out of reach, unrealistic and exaggerated at times, it is all through a metaphor and symbolistic way that leads us back to our own life with a want to be better. Everything in the story seems to be relative back to our own lives, no matter how big or small the connection, and leaves us with lessons to remember such as not to fear, and to do what makes us happy in the long run. Rather than this story being unrealistic, it is merely just an allegory, with everything relative to our own lives. This results in a feeling to do better and make better things of ourselves throughout our lives. If everyone were to sincerely work to make things better in their own lives, the world would become a better place and that's what makes The Alchemist a book worth the read.

B008B0QH5S|5.0|The 11th album by the Pet Shop Boys, it shows that, nearly 27 years after their first album and over 30 years together, they can still create excellent music. I consider all PSB albums \\"masterpieces in their own right\\", and this is no exception. Sure, I had my doubts when the producer was announced, but it seems PSB is so impeccable, nothing can ruin them, so I feel sheepish for doubting them. But I'm very glad I was wrong.<br /><br />The album is a great pleasure to listen to from start to finish, with a few hitches here and there. Some lyrics can sound a bit silly from time to time, but they won't detract from the experience, not unless you absolutely want to nitpick at it. After listening to the album as a whole, I found it interesting, in retrospect, that \\"Invisible\\" was released to give a sample of the sound of the album. Quite honestly, the album is not all slow and plodding, but it's also not completely fast. It has an interesting mixture of musical themes, which actually ends up working well.<br /><br />To start, \\"Leaving\\" is an upbeat sounding pop song with a catchy chorus, but whose lyrical content morphs from a simple tale of love lost to how \\"the dead is still with us\\" and so on. Very interesting lyrical content. 5/5<br />\\"Invisible\\" is the slow, prodding, but somber song, which evokes more sentiment than somnolence. It has this air of loneliness and sorrow that is present in every note of the song, whose lyrical content is up for debate, until Neil Tennant decides to explain to us what it is. Still, we will probably come up with our own meaning, as we always do. 5/5<br />\\"Winner\\" is the upbeat song with a nice message, if not a bit corny, and very different from the usual PSB stuff; but isn't that the great thing, that the Boys can be so varied? Some of the lyrics can be seen as corny, but the song is still oddly catchy anyways. 4/5<br />\\"Your Early Stuff\\" is a slow song, lyrically \\"YWIWM\\" redux, just MUCH worse, but since that was 7 albums ago, maybe it's time to revisit the theme. It's basically a satire of those who have long since written them off, perhaps even since the days of \\"Actually\\", who only care about their \\"early stuff\\", and wonder what they're doing now. It's a fairly short song, but other than that, not bad. 4/5<br />\\"A Face Like That\\" is the ultimate pop song on the album, very PSB sounding. Those seeking pure \\"dance PSB\\" should be very happy with this. 5/5<br />\\"Breathing Space\\" is a slow, gentle song, unlike Invisible. 4/5<br />\\"Ego Music\\" is a fast song, and lyrically something of a \\"HCYE(X)TBTS\\" redux. It's oddly addictive, even with it's somewhat silly \\"me x 4, yes x 4, you x 4, no x4\\" chorus. 4/5<br />\\"Hold On\\" is another inspirational sounding song, but one of these is enough. This song sounds like a showtune song, especially with it's many singers. Fairly dismissible, especially for a PSB song. 2/5<br />\\"Give It A Go\\" is reminiscent of a number of simple PSB songs, a very nice addition. 4/5<br />\\"Memory Of The Future\\" is a bit of a slow, but electropop, song. The melody sounds, at times, disjointed, but listening to it as a whole, it does come together. 5/5<br />\\"Everything Means Something\\" is a harder \\"rock\\" sounding track, whose chorus deviates from the normal 4/4 time signature, while the rest of the song stays in it, becoming (I believe) 6/4, which only means that it goes from \\"1,2,3,4 repeat\\" to \\"1,2,3,4,5,6 repeat\\". A very interesting track overall, very nice addition. 5/5.<br />\\"Requiem In Denim And Leopardskin\\" is a mid tempo, somewhat calypso sounding song, although not exactly. Very simple, at times a bit compex, but overall a nice song with interesting lyrical content, and a VERY interesting title. 4/5.<br /><br />Elysium is a very nice piece of work by PSB, delivering what you would expect; great music, great lyrics. After so long, they've still got it; their \\"recent\\" stuff is still as good, if not better than, their \\"early\\" stuff. But when it's all pretty much one big canon ofmasterpiece after masterpiece, it's all relative from there on out.<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed this album and most everything it had to offer, just like the albums before it, and I look forward to what the future holds for the Boys after Elysium.

If you do not learn something with this book, perhaps you were not meant to be one of those that read.<br /><br />When he argues against economists on the right, he often gives two sides to his arguments. One is, perhaps the conservative economists don't understand certain facts which he then elucidates. The other argument is that some on the right are just being deceptive on purpose.<br /><br />Krugman gives prescriptions, both government spending programs and monetary prescriptions that could decrease the unemployment rate by several percent right now. One would be that if Obama wins the election, he should fire the guy who refuses to let the federal government reduce mortgage principals and monthly payments for people that are on the verge of defaulting or are underwater in their mortgages.

0316017930|5.0|In this book, Malcolm Gladwell asks how highly-successful people become successful. He plays against the belief that these people are unusually talented or hard-working, though of course they are that too. Instead, he focuses on the social background, the legacies, and the luck that brought people success.  Being born in January makes for good hockey players, being born to Jewish Eastern European garment workers in New York in the early 1930s makes for successful mergers-and-acquisitions lawyers, and 17% of the richest people in all of human history were Americans born within nine years of one another in the mid-nineteenth century. He also looks at how some geniuses fail to succeed, for a lack of opportunity, social networks, or class background.<br /><br />Some chapters wander off the main theme but are interesting in their own way, looking at how traditional Korean society causes plane crashes or the Scots Irish tradition leads to family feuds. And a few chapters fell flat for me, like rice paddies and math.  But overall, it's a continually-fascinating book, well-written and fast-paced.<br /><br />All the evidence is anecdotal, so if you think in social-scientific terms you are likely to be frustrated. That's part of my frustration with some of the chapters. However, the anecdotes are sufficiently interesting that I am happy to forgive - the book is better at provoking than persuading.<br /><br />The book also comes with a reminder that many Americans seem to need: yes, hard work is necessary for success, but no person is an island. Society shapes whether you can work hard, whether your hard work can succeed, and sometimes how your hard work goes for naught. Gladwell's bottom line is worth remembering: we can do better.

B004EBT5CU|3.0|I can't fault Adele's material at all.  Her bluesy and soulful style is addictive and seductive.  Like the \\"Adele 19\\" album, \\"Adele 21\\" is packed to the gills with great music.<br /><br />It's the sound quality that is horrible.  Regardless, if which format you buy this album (or the previous one) on, the same fatiguing sound quality rears its ugly face because the entire album has been butchered by the recording engineers' use of dynamic limiting.  This is the extreme electronic compression of the sound's dynamic range - the compression of the difference between the softest and loudest passages of the music.  What we end up with is a stuffy, congested sound quality that is very loud.  Drums sound like they're being struck by cotton candy rather than drumsticks.  Adele's vocals are also ruined by this because it sounds like she is actually singing with the microphone in her mouth!  I honestly can't listen to more than one song at a time.<br /><br />XL Recordings, the recording label for both Adele's albums, is no stranger to the practice of limiting.  They obviously won't change their ways and they don't have any incentive to since both albums have been certified multi-platinum.  But Adele can do herself and us a favor in the future and sign a contract with a label that cares about good sound.  The Verve or Blue Note labels would do her (and her listeners) good.

B0018QOIXU|3.0|The title of this review really sums up the review nicely.  But I really should elaborate.<br /><br />After noticing that Adele won six Grammy Awards including the Album of The Year Award, I was curious about whether Adele's material was just hype.  No hype.  With Adele's one-of-a-kind bluesy vocal style it was no wonder she could connect with both older listeners and younger listeners (who obviously know she stands out among other current artists).  Nearly every one of the songs on \\"Adele 19\\" is genuinely sung from Adele's soul.  So, I loved the music.<br /><br />Being a vinyl fan, my wife bought the LPs for both Adele 19 and Adele 21 ($40 total).  The LP itself was more massive (thicker) than your average LP.  This is normally a good thing.  But the first sign of trouble was when I eyeballed the surface of the LP.  It had quite a few dips.  I haven't seen this defect for a long time on LPs.  Whoever pressed the original lacquer didn't know what the hell he was doing.  The result was high surface noise - those \\"ticks\\" and \\"pops\\" that a lot of older people are familiar with.  Some listeners may mistake this defect to be the stereotypical sound of vinyl.  But make no mistake: a great pressing is usually so quiet that all you hear is music.  The second negative was the heavy limiting (extreme compression in volume between the softest and loudest passages) applied to the recording itself.  Whether it was done during the original recording, the mixing stage, the mastering or a combination of these stages is not clear.  But the sound is so congested, thick, loud, stuffy, and lacking percussive punch that it is absolutely fatiguing.  On top of this, \\"Chasing Pavements\\" and \\"Melt My Heart To Stone\\" were the worst offenders of sound quality because Adele's vocals were over-recorded to the point of digital clipping; this is distortion in its worst form and it was so severe that I thought the turntable's cartridge was mistracking.  I should have known that it wasn't the cartridge because it plays through anything with ease.<br /><br />So I thought the CD was going to be an improvement.  Another mistake!  The distortion on the vocals for the same two bands were still there and the extreme limiting on the dynamic range (squashed volume difference between the softest and loudest passages of the music) was just as bad.  I honestly can't listen to more than one song at a time on this album.  That's a real shame because Adele deserves better and so do we!<br /><br />I met a recording engineer who once told me that the sound quality of any music can make or break an artists album.  I didn't believe it then and I don't believe it now.  Both Adele's albums have been certified multi-platinum.  So it seems that most people dont' care about sound quality.  I might be among the very few who care to listen on an audio component system rather than a little docking station.<br /><br />Sad.  Very sad.

B008LZHA3G|5.0|By all that's holy, Dylan's done it again.<br /><br />With Tempest, Bob Dylan continues to mine the vein of creativity that began with 1997s Time Out of Mind.  The man often called the \\"Voice of a Generation\\" is actually, like his friend, the late Johnny Cash, an embodiment of American song.  Folk, early rock `n' roll, 1940s and 50s pop, and jump blues supply the musical foundation.  The opening track, \\"Duquesne Whistle,\\" at first sounds like a field recording from the early twentieth century, something that might have been played at a vaudeville show.  Then the band fully kicks in and Dylan joins the proceedings, sounding a little bit like Louis Armstrong.  It is a jaunty opening for an album with lyrics that are quite often dark, laced with humorous twists and gruesome details.<br /><br />Lyrically, Dylan proves again why he remains one of the most highly regarded songwriters of all time.  He lays out lines in Tempest's ten songs that can leave a listener laughing, shocked, stunned, and wondering, sometimes all in the same song.  Dylan borrows liberally from other sources.  William Blake, The Beatles, and the Bible all get referenced, and there are sly nods to Bob Marley, Paul Simon, Vern Gosdin, and countless others scattered throughout.  The song \\"Long and Wasted Years,\\" with its vintage rock groove and spoken vocals, is reminiscent of the work of poet John Trudell, for whom Dylan has expressed an appreciation.  The songs manage to sound both timeless and new.<br /><br />There are many high points on Tempest, and no lows.  The song \\"Pay in Blood\\" is quite simply astounding, aggressive, and angry.  Bob literally barks out the lyrics; at the beginning of the song, he is almost incomprehensible.  He spews wicked humor (\\"You've got the same eyes that your mother does.  If only you could prove who your father was\\"), road-weary fatalism (\\"But, I'll play this hand whether I like it or not\\"), and cranky brutality (\\"Come here, I'll break your lousy head\\"). At the end ofeach verse, Dylan growls, \\"I pay in blood, but not my own.\\"  It is a towering achievement, even in the formidable Dylan canon.<br /><br />The title track has already garnered much attention.  It is a nearly fourteen minute fictionalized account of the sinking of the Titanic, complete with Leonardo DiCaprio and murderous fights.  It has been declared a masterpiece by some and a tedious dirge by others.  Masterpiece or not, it is a compelling story, and it is a highlight of the album.  Another highlight is \\"Tin Angel,\\" a folk melody coupled with some graphic violence (\\"Bleeding from the mouth, he's as good as dead\\").<br /><br />Dylan's voice has become more frayed than many could imagine, but he uses it well in service to these songs.  He snarls, rasps, and croons; at times, he sounds downright playful.  It is a battered instrument, but it still plays.<br /><br />In an interview from long ago, the late folk singer Phil Ochs commented on laughing when he first heard Highway 61 Revisited because the album was \\"impossibly good.\\"  It is easy to get that same feeling listening to Tempest: in the fifteen years since Time Out of Mind re-established him as one of the most formidable talents on our landscape, Dylan continues to impress, surprise, and remain \\"impossibly good.\\"  Essential listening.

B00514HDNW|2.0|I Am Legend by Richard Matheson is a book about a lone human survivor in a world populated with disease stricken vampires. I'm sure many people did not hear about this story until they've watched the movie starring Will Smith. Not surprisingly, I am one of those people. However, I Am Legend was a novel published back in 1954! Of course the story got popularized and a couple of movies was actually created based on the novel. With that being said, I didn't really like the movie version and I don't even remember if I finished it or not. I decided to give the book a try since there has been so much talk about it in the past. At a little over 300 pages, it wasn't that big of a risk. Upon completion though, I still find myself not really liking the story all that much. In fact, I was pretty close to not wanting to finish the book just like with the movie.<br /><br />The story is simple. Robert Neville believes that he is the last human in a world infested with vampires. Somehow he is immune to the plague that managed to transform all other humans into vampire-like creatures and make his world a living hell. As the story progresses, Robert decides to try and solve the mystery of what makes these creatures tick and why it is that they are afraid of garlic and the holy cross.Throughout the story, Robert will have self doubts as if you think about it and put yourself in his position, why should he even care? He is the last surviving human and its not like he will make a difference even if he finds out the truth about the whole affair.<br /><br />One of the biggest disappointment I had with I Am Legend is that Robert is just so boring! Robert spends most of his days fortifying his home against the vampires and once night comes, he locks himself in his home and drinks himself to a stupor to try and forget about his situation. This pretty much continues until about the first 40% of the book. Now, I do understand that this is a depressing story and as such, the character should also follow along those guidelines. It just wasn't that fun to read. For the last 60% or so of the book, things do get a bit more interesting once Robert actually does his best to find out the reasoning for the plague and how it affects humans. While reading, I was so anxious for them to explain the \\"legend\\" part. Towards the end, once I realized what it actually meant, it did give me some chills.<br /><br />Upon completion of the book, I just can't help it but to think of how this story could be rewritten for the modern day era. Robert practically has the whole Los Angeles to himself and it felt not much was accomplished. It would have been awesome to read about Roberts adventures when he goes foraging for food and supplies throughout the city. I also wished more emphasis was being put on the vampires. At certain times, they seem more like mindless zombies to me than vampires. To give credit where its due though, the author did successfully create a very depressing atmosphere and I think that was his intention. Its not so much about the action or killing but more so about human weakness. My final conclusion is that I Am Legend is very average and does not really deserve the hype it did.lso follow along those guidelines. It just wasn't that fun to read. 1451627297|5.0|I've read every Stephen King novel (except the Gunslinger novels which, after reading the first one held no interest to me) and I long ago noticed a pattern in his releases. Since his 70's masterpieces, he releases one great book every 5 years:<br />81 - Cujo<br />86 - It<br />91 - Needful Things<br />96 - Desperation<br />2001 - Dreamcatcher<br />2006 - Cell<br />2011 - 11/22/63<br /><br />Amazing how that has transpired. My own opinion of course. Sure, he's had some good books in between (Pet Sematary, Misery,  The Green Mile, Different Seasons, plus a couple of the earlier short story collections) but a fair bit of crap also. Yet somehow he remains my favourite author.  The one great book every five outweigh the 4-5 lesser ones in-between.<br /><br />This is the first SK novel I've reviewed in here and am doing so because I felt I owed it to what was a truly great book. I loved this. I read it at work in 20-30 minute shifts when I could get away with it (no I'm not a slacker and yes, that sort of this IS frowned upon here) it was so good. I'm not from the USA and JFK and his death (and legacy) holds no great meaning to me other than it's some tragedy that happened in `the past'. I was not overly interested in the subject matter of this book and wasn't looking forward to reading it. Reading it wasn't a high priority to me, it was something that I would `just do' at some point given it was a King novel and I read all King novels. After the thousand pages of rubbish that was Under The Dome and the underwhelming fare that SK has released of late, 730 pages of JFK was not an appealing prospect. I remember actually uttering a sigh of disappointment after seeing how long this one was when I picked it up.<br /><br />How wrong was I. I admit it. I judged a book by its cover - or in that instant (and a bit of recent past history to compare it to), its length.<br /><br />11.22.63 is thoroughly enjoyable and a reminder, for me at least, just how brilliant SK is.<br /><br />Amajor issue with many previous SK books (mainly the 80's ones) has been the endings. So much time is spent building up characters and scenarios only to have a ridiculous climax. The stories were so big that they were never going to be able to be concluded with any great satisfaction. Yes I'm a fan and have been since I was old enough to read his work. I'm not a one-eyed fan that loves everything he does and can't take others criticising him. His work has been so varied that no King fan could claim to love everything he's written. Anyway, there's no stupid ending here. It's as it should be (though perhaps as expected) but satisfying all the same.<br /><br />Time travel can be hit and miss. You pretty much always just have to go with it and its true here. However, the protagonist is aware of his actions (even small ones) at the start but the further he goes the less he seems to think about the consequences. In reality (if you have such a thing with time travel) even the smallest action can have massive consequences. Even sneezing near someone could have a dire effect - if you think about it. If you can't get past that side of things then you won't enjoy this (or any other time travel story) and probably won't be reading this anyway....<br /><br />I recall at the start of the novel thinking (or guessing) how I would like things to transpire in the set up and to my satisfaction, they did. I loved the `reset' scenario. I'm not sure if that's been done before by a previous writer but it's an interesting and important part of the book.<br /><br />The pacing for the first third is spot on. A chunk could be taken out of the middle section when things begin to slow down (about the time Oswald starts to become the focus), though I enjoyed the time spent in Jodie. The last third is fast paced as time starts to `catch up' with our protagonist.<br /><br />As for characters, they're a good bunch. Most are interesting but, of course, have the usual King `twang' and the at times somewhat annoying speech patterns. You also get the usual tangents of someone's backstory that don't always necessarily drive the story forward. Most here are worthwhile though but, as is often the case with SK, could be trimmed back a bit.<br /><br />So, in conclusion, 11.22.63 is King's latest 5 year semi work of genius. Not one he'll be remembered for and probably won't make many Top Ten King Best Of's but it's on mine and to me, that's what matters. I hope you get the enjoyment out of it that I did and are reminded of the brilliance SK is capable of.

A very salty yet groovy track.<br /><br />Freedom at 21 - Odd meter percussion that echo, a riff that reminds you of 7 Nation Army takes you on a sonic journey that plays with the stereo effect. Instead of just laying in the normal stereo field your left and right ear are treated to their own different solos, and in some place, lyrics. This song is best listened to with headphones!<br /><br />Love Interruption - An excellent visual picture over folk guitar. To me, this is kind of the sleeper song on the album but the glue in the transition to the end of the project.<br /><br />I'm Shakin' - a great throw back to a bluesy kind of song that will get you moving again, clapping your hands and bobbing your head.<br /><br />Trash Tongue Talker - another groovy blues song that features a layed back guitar used sparingly and rhythmically with some tastey throw back riffs thrown in for the true music afficianado.<br /><br />Hip (Eponymous) Poor Boy - this song is kind of a bridge between blues and bluegrass that show the musical roots of Jack's past.<br /><br />I Guess I Should Go To Sleep - upright bass, complex chords, tripled vocals (baritone, soprano, tenor) singing in unison... great song.<br /><br />On and On and On -  After showing us rock, blues, bluegrass, this song starts off with a mellow smooth jazz groove that lulls you into something that reminded me of Tori Amos. At the end, the song seems like it could ramp up into some chaotic frenzy, but holds back.<br /><br />Rake Me with You When You Go - is my favorite track on the CD and a great way to end this journey. Finally, the Jack White guitar and hurried vocals come in which are doubled and layered with a frenetic urgency that makes you wonder if the notes sung by each person were improvised or felt in the moment. This song left me asking for 1 more!<br /><br />I highly recommend this CD and that you enjoy the journey.

He doesn't prove that potatoes are bad or actually have a disastrous effect on insulin like sugar or processed flours might.<br /><br />3.  The author doesn't take into consideration the work of Dr. McDougall who proposes a largely starch-based diet (ie., rice, potatoes, corn, beans etc) supplemented with vegetables and fruits.  Dr. McDougall has thousands of people who have lost lots of weight and kept it off with his diet.  It shares similarities with Gary Taube's suggestions in that it restricts sugars and processed flours.  But McDougall promotes whole, natural starches (rice, potatoes, corn, etc).  My take is that whole, natural starches might boost insulin some but in the range where it's healthy and thus doesn't promote the fat-storing activities that a unnatural spike in insulin would produce with artificially concentrated foods (ie., sugar, refined flours, etc).  For those interested, read McDougall's book and check out the reviews:  [[ASIN:1609613937 The Starch Solution: Eat the Foods You Love, Regain Your Health, and Lose the Weight for Good!]].<br /><br />4.  Gary Taube even says it's ok to have artificial sweeteners in moderation.  I know this is off topic a bit, but he really should look at the evidence out there of the disastrous health effects of artificial sweeteners.  This really makes me doubt his medical expertise.<br /><br />Overall, his book suggests a diet heavy in fat, followed by protein, followed by vegetables, follow by some fruit.  It could work for some.  But I don't think he lays out a strong enough case to reject McDougall's starch solution approach.  Or if you do some research, google \\"The Rice Diet Solution\\".  There are other starch-focused diet solutions that many find helpful.  If Taube's claims were true, then those doing mostly starch-based diets (ie., McDougall's) wouldn't be able to lose weight and keep it off.<br /><br />Taube needs to re-examine and re-research the way he groups carbs together and scapegoats even the good whole, natural starches (ie., rice, potatoes, corn, beans, etc).

0061122416|3.0|The Alchemist is a stimulating book for those who choose to open their mind to many abstract views of the world around them. Although jumbled and difficult to fully understand, the Alchemist asks the reader to analyze the workings of the world. A book that makes you think is a book worth reading. In the book, Paulo Coelho puts forth many of his religious beliefs and gives the reader an opportunity to embrace them and evaluate the amount of truth behind them. Some of these beliefs include aspects of fate, dreams, and most often, God, making the reader's acceptance of such things key to their understanding of them.  One such lesson is chasing one's dreams, or rather Personal Legends as the book puts it. Coelho simplifies the idea of chasing dreams, telling the reader that when they do so, the whole universe conspires to help them. This makes the message hard for the reader to relate to, as we all know \\"chasing your dreams\\" is never an easy process, in contrast with the book. The amount of value the novel puts on pursuing dreams is truly inspiring though, encouraging the reader that dreams will lead to fulfillment. Through probing the text, and trying to understand the author, one may find the morals of this book powerful truths that are applicable to their own lives, the reason I value it as a worthy book.<br />Throughout the novel, Coelho piles life lessons high upon each other and bombards the reader with them, making it difficult to focus on the main message. It is exemplified when Melchizedek speaks to Santiago in the plaza, touching on Personal Legends, the Soul of the World, the world's greatest lie, and other aspects of the world without going into depth, making the reader wonder what aspects they should focus on and try to understand. His seemingly scatterbrained writing makes the most important messages that much harder to pick out and comprehend. Yet if one is patient enough to sort through the jumble of writing that Coehlo presents, and keeps an unbiased, untainted mind, the viewpoint explained is one worth knowing.

B008LZHA3G|3.0|Bob Dylan is on cruise control. At the age of 71, he still tours relentlessly. And he records new albums about as quickly as your great-grandfather can peruse his collection of pre-prohibition vinyl records. He's like an old, rickety car making its way down the highway on fumes, puttering on with no sign of what'll give first: the engine, or that old tape-deck.<br /><br />Dylan said that Tempest would be a wild ride, but I never really believed it. And despite some stellar early reviews, I don't think Tempest is a return to form the same way that Time Out of Mind. But it is a continuation in that same general direction, picking off where subsequent lesser-albums like Love and Theft and Modern Times left off.<br /><br />The album opener, \\"Duquesne Whistle\\", is a typical 21st Cenutry Dylan opener: a strong, catchy song with just enough lyrical curiosity to latch onto,  \\"Don't you hear that duquesne whistle blowing/ Blowing like the sky's gonna blow apart/ You're the only thing alive that keeps me going/ you're like a time-bomb to my heart\\" Those apocalyptic images make up one of Tempest's many motifs. The third track, \\"Narrow Way\\", features a burning White House, an empty cup, fighting in the streets and seeing someone both \\"buried\\" and dug-up. \\"You won't get out of here unscarred\\" Dylan says, before eventually waxing romantic.  Later in \\"Pay in Blood\\" Dylan tells us, in a particularly ghoulish bark, \\"I came to bury/ and not to praise\\". Toward the end of the record, there's a long ballad about the Titanic sinking (it's the title track). Like another one of Dylan's long and uneventful ballads, \\"Joey\\" from 1976's Desire, \\"Tempest\\" is tiresome and of questionable sentiment-- and makes reference to \\"Leo and his sketchbook\\". Dylan croons about the masses drowning amid the Titanic's extravagance, but it's hard to tell whether he actually cares. That ballad, clocking in at just over 13 minutes, is neither as good nor as coherent as the one which precedes it: a nine minute song called \\"Tin Angel\\" which deals with themes of personal struggle, salvation, and reformation and is the closest  Dylan ever gets to classics like \\"Hollis Brown\\".<br /><br />The most difficult song to devour is \\"Roll On John\\", which is either a tribute or mockery of John Lennon's legacy.  Dylan, from the present, warns John in the past, and smugly characterizes him as the singer who always played \\"to the cheap seats\\" and dressed in rags \\"like any other slave\\"; and then Dylan wails \\"I heard the news today, oh boy\\" and other Beatles lyrics, and makes wild exaggerations like \\"there was no more Joy\\" after Lennon died; and furthermore advises John to pass-over into that land where \\"the Buffalo roam\\". On one hand, Dylan does seem genuinely miss the influence of one of his greatest peers. On the other hand, I can't help but hear a few jabs to a rival between the lines of commemoration, especially given the extravagant way the lyrics pile-on praise and bizarre, sometimes outright goofy, imagery. Or perhaps the topic strikes too close to home for Dylan to handle. Whatever the case, \\"Roll On John\\" is the strangest, and in my opinion worst, song of the album.<br /><br />Ultimately, Dylan is Dylan, and he puts out whatever music he wants to. There are those among us who would sacrifice all the musical world in exchange for the privilege to listen to Bob Dylan's music, and those of us who wouldn't. After hearing Tempest, I still belong to the first group.<br /><br />Like Shakespeare's play of the same name, Tempest isn't Dylan's best or most enduring work and it might just be his last. But if that's how things wind up, it's not cause Dylan has planned it that way. \\"I ain't dead yet/ my bell still rings/ I keep my fingers crossed\\" says Dylan in another track, \\"Early Roman Kings\\". And like that rickety car, he seems to just keep on going.ng called \\"Tin Angel\\" which deals with themes of personal struggle, salvation, and reformation and is the closest  Dylan ever gets to classics like \\"Hollis Brown\\".<br /><br />The most difficult song to devour is \\"Roll On John\\", which is either a tribute or mockery of John Lennon's legacy. 0385344228|4.0|Parents will do whatever it takes to protect their children from physical harm or to defend them from anyone, or anything, that otherwise threatens their wellbeing.  That is a given; it is just what we do - even for our adult children.  But what if a parent suspects that a son or daughter might have committed a violent crime against someone else's child?  And what if that parent is the District Attorney charged with prosecuting the very crime his son has been accused of committing?<br /><br />First District Attorney Andy Barber, facing precisely that \\"what if\\" question, never hesitates.  Jacob is the most important thing in Andy's life, and if Andy has to place his own future in jeopardy in order to save his son's life, well, that is what he will do.<br /><br />One of Jacob Barber's classmates, a 14-year-old boy, has been found stabbed to death along one of the walkways of the park that numerous students cross on their way to the middle school every morning.  Andy Barber, once he gets over the shock of such a thing happening so close to home, is determined to find and prosecute the murderer as quickly as possible.  He recognizes the fear and unease of his friends and neighbors and believes that moving quickly will help restore the community's sense of security and normalcy.<br /><br />However, Andy and Laurie Barber receive an even bigger shock when their son is arrested for the murder and Andy is forced to take a leave of absence from the District Attorney's office.  As the investigation evolves, the Barbers and the parents of the dead boy will learn things about their sons they never could have imagined.  They will also learn things about themselves and their neighbors that are almost as disturbing as what is revealed about the boys.<br /><br />Defending Jacob is a classic courtroom thriller that will remind the reader of earlier novels like Presumed Innocent and A Time to Kill, two other books that caught the imaginations of readers and sold in huge numbers.  And like the best of his predecessors, William Landay focus on nicely developed characters, plot twists, and a major surprise or two near the end to create a memorable story.  But, even though there are recognizable similarities between Jacob Barber's case and some recent real-world teen-murderers, Defending Jacob is as much a study of family dynamics as it is a legal thriller.<br /><br />The three members of the Barber family, ostracized and hated by their neighbors - every one of whom believe Jacob is the killer - have only each other for support and comfort.  But, as the pressure of the trial mounts, with more and more evidence pointing to Jacob's guilt, Andy and Laurie begin to sense that their son's emotional response to his arrest is odd.  The family's survival is in doubt in more ways than one.<br /><br />Surprisingly, the most memorable character in Defending Jacob is Jacob's grandfather, a man who moves into and out of the picture as the plot develops.  The man's pure evilness certainly makes a lasting impression; for me, one even more striking than that made by Andy Barber's decision to place his son's life above the very justice system he has spent a lifetime serving.<br /><br />Defending Jacob is probably a little bit over-hyped but it has its moments and will certainly be enjoyed by courtroom drama fans. 0547564651|5.0|Following the footsteps of Jonathan Kozol, Paul Tough employs his significant storytelling abilities to help readers see and feel the plight of children, families and communities trapped in cycles of failure and poverty. How Children Succeed challenges some conventional wisdom on causes of failure (poverty, teacher quality) and contends that nurturing character in children and young adults is the key to success. As a former NYC Teaching Fellow who has lived and worked in multiple communities of cyclical poverty, I'm convinced that Tough has nailed some critical pieces of breaking those cycles.<br /><br />Here is the argument in brief:<br />==============================<br />There exists in our society a troubling and growing achievement gap between the have and the have-nots. The cause of that gap is neither merely poverty nor IQ, but a specific set of non-cognitive skills including executive function and conscientiousness, which Tough calls \\"character.\\" Children who acquire these skills can break historic cyclical patterns of failure.<br /><br />Malleability of Character and Intelligence<br />==========================================<br />Whereas IQ is hardly malleable, executive function and character strengths - specifically grit, self-control, zest, social intelligence, gratitude, optimism, curiosity and conscientiousness - are far more malleable. These skills are better predictors of academic performance and educational achievement than IQ and therefore ought to be the direct target of interventions.<br /><br />Attachment and Lifelong Health<br />==============================<br />Tough sees two key areas of influence for those who care for those trapped in cycles of poverty. The first is secure early attachment to parents. \\"The effect of good parenting is not just emotional or psychological, the neuroscientists say; it is biochemical\\" (28). Specifically, children who experience high levels of stress but NOT responsive and nurturing parents suffer from a range of lifelong health and mental health issues. However, \\"When mothers scored high on measures of responsiveness, the impact of those environmental factors on their children seemed to almost disappear\\" (32). Tough cites one study in which \\"early parental care predicted which students would graduate even more reliably than IQ or achievement test scores\\" (36). Importantly, interventions that focus on promoting stronger parent-child relationships in high risk groups (including one in which just 1 of 137 infants studied demonstrated secure attachment at the outset) have shown promising impact. Of the 137 children in the study, 61% of those in the treatment group formed secure attachment by age 2, compared with only 2% of the control group.<br /><br />Adolescent Character Formation<br />==============================<br />Paul Tough highlights the work of school and support programs that intentionally focus on forming the character strength habits that enable children to learn well in schools, form healthy relationships, and avoid the destructive decisions and behavior patterns modeled in their communities. Here, too, Tough sees a ray of hope. Just as early intervention with parents and young children yields wide ranging benefits for families in poverty, so character interventions in adolescence can and do enable young adults surrounded by cycles of poverty to learn self-control, perseverance and focus that are critical for escaping the gravitational pull of their communities.<br /><br />Why You Should Read This Book<br />=============================<br />Paul Tough is tackling one of the most challenging - and contentious - issues of our time. His analysis will offend those who tend to blame poverty predominantly on the irresponsible choices of the poor by showing just how powerful the cyclical, environmental pressures are on children raised in these communities. His work is just as challenging to those who think that those trapped in cycles of poverty are mere victimsof their environment who bear no responsibility for their decisions. Tough shows compellingly that parents and children in poverty can and do overcome the powerful environmental forces of their communities - and that this is a beautiful and essential component of breaking cyclical poverty. His call is for those with education and influence - the kinds of people who read books like his - to demonstrate motivation and volition (two components of character formation he extols) to recognize, celebrate, and nurture the character of children and families in poverty.<br /><br />Graham Scharf<br />Author, The Apprenticeship of Being Human: Why Early Childhood Parenting Matters to Everyone<br />[...]of their environment who bear no responsibility for their decisions. Tough shows compellingly that parents and children in poverty can and do overcome the powerful environmental forces of their communities - and that this is a beautiful and essential component of breaking cyclical poverty. His call is for those with education and influence - the kinds of people who read books like his - to demonstrate motivation and volition (two components of character formation he extols) to recognize, celebrate, and nurture the character of children and families in poverty.<br /><br />Graham Scharf<br />Author, The Apprenticeship of Being Human: Why Early Childhood Parenting Matters to Everyone<br />[...]

B001OQCV2E|1.0|The Book of Revelation this is NOT for those likely to assume the movie is an adequate description of events portrayed there.  It is better viewed as hyperbolic post-modernism in which Nostradamas quatrains are integrated into a fixed calendar endpoint a la Mayan.  So, it's the atypical neutrino behavior that is behind the Earth's crust/core overheating and subsequent liquifaction/shift?  Nonscientists in the audience can be duped into believing this is a plausible scenario which is why Hollywood uses it as its point of departure.<br /><br />And, how about the competing themes in the movie; one espoused by the geologist in which 'representative genetic stock is selected for salvation' and the other by the crass former US presidential Chief of Staff '1 billion euros per ticket, after all we're not running a charity'?  This interplay/confusion mars the script in that the audience is left to labor under a false dichotomy pressurized by the faux &#34;crisis&#34; created by the dreaded 'End of the World'.  In fact, no one knows how this interplay would work out in fact; yet, we have examples from our own history from both 9.11.2001 response and hurricane Katrina.  Likely, short term, the fallen nature of man would be plainly evident, but what if altruism was removed entirely from the equation?  This is the view of Revelation, when fallen man is left completely to his/her own devices without the restraint of altruistic urges.<br /><br />The only couple of whimsical touches in the movie is casting the actual Buddist llama of Nepal to represent himself and the attempt on the part of common Tibetans to claim a right of stowaway passage abord an exclusive, wealth- and superiority-driven Ark.

This book is brilliantly written, set in the time of the holocaust and strangely enough the hero is not one of the opressed but rather an opressor.  What I liked about this book was how vividly you felt their life down to bites of food in a time when food was scarce. This is not a preachy book either. It still manages to be an entertaining thriller.
